10003{_}555{_}We had many visitors after breakfast, all much excited, wondering what the next step would be--if the Chambers would be dissolved, the marshal trying to impose a cabinet of the Right or perhaps form another moderate liberal cabinet without Jules Simon, but retaining some of his ministers.
10003{_}556{_}It was my reception afternoon, and while I was sitting quietly in my drawing-room talking to some of my friends, making plans for the summer, quite pleased to have W. to myself again, the butler hurried into the room telling me that the Marechale de MacMahon was on the stairs, coming to make me a visit.
10003{_}557{_}I was very much surprised, as she never came to see me.
10003{_}558{_}We met very rarely, except on official occasions, and she made no secret of her dislike to the official Republican ladies (but she was always absolutely correct if not enthusiastic).
10003{_}559{_}I had just time to get to the head of the stairs to receive her.
10003{_}560{_}She was very amiable, a little embarrassed, took a cup of tea--said the marshal was very sorry to part with W., he had never had any trouble or disagreement with him of any kind, but that it was impossible to go on with a cabinet when neither party had any confidence in the other.
10003{_}561{_}I quite agreed, said it was the fortunes of war; I hoped the marshal would find another premier who would be more sympathetic with him, and then we talked of other things.
10003{_}562{_}My friends were quite amused.
10003{_}563{_}One of them, Marquise de T., knew the Marechale quite well, and said she was going to ask her if she was obliged to make visites de condoleance to the wives of all the fallen ministers.
10003{_}564{_}W. was rather astonished when I told him who had come to tea with me, and thought the conversation must have been difficult.
10003{_}565{_}I told him, not at all, once the necessary phrases about the departing ministers were over.
10003{_}566{_}The piano was open, music littered about; she was fond of music and she admired very much a portrait of father as a boy in the Harrow dress, asked who it was and what the dress was.
10003{_}567{_}She was a perfect woman of the world, and no one was uncomfortable.
10003{_}568{_}It seemed quite strange and very pleasant to take up my old life again after two years of public life.
10003{_}569{_}W. breakfasted at home, went to the Senate every day and to the Institute on Fridays and we dined with our friends and had small dinners in our own house instead of official banquets at all the ministries (usually from Potel and Chabot at so much a head).
10003{_}570{_}Politics were very lively all summer.
10003{_}571{_}The Chambers were dissolved almost at once after the constitution of the new cabinet, presided over by the Duc de Broglie.
10003{_}572{_}It was evident from the first moment that the new ministry wouldn't, couldn't live.
10003{_}573{_}(The Duc de Broglie was quite aware of the fact.
10003{_}574{_}His first words on taking office were: "On nous a jetes a l'eau, maintenant il faut nager.")
10003{_}575{_}He made a very good fight, but he had that worst of all faults for a leader, he was unpopular.
10003{_}576{_}He was a brilliant, cultured speaker, but had a curt, dictatorial manner, with an air always of looking down upon his public.
10003{_}577{_}So different from his colleague, the Duc Decazes, whose charming, courteous manners and nice blue eyes made him friends even among his adversaries.
10003{_}578{_}There is a well-known story told of the two dukes which shows exactly the personality of the men.
10003{_}579{_}Some one, a deputy I think, wanted something very much which either of the gentlemen could give.
10003{_}580{_}He went first to the Duc Decazes, then Minister of Foreign Affairs, who received him charmingly, was most kind and courteous, but didn't do what the man wanted.
10003{_}581{_}He then went to the Duc de Broglie, President du Conseil, who was busy, received him very curtly, cut short his explanations, and was in fact extremely disagreeable but did the thing, and the man loved Decazes and hated de Broglie.
10003{_}582{_}All sorts of rumours were afloat; we used to hear the wildest stories and plans.
10003{_}583{_}One day W. came in looking rather preoccupied.
10003{_}584{_}There was an idea that the Right were going to take most stringent measures, arrest all the ministers, members of Jules Simon's cabinet, many of the prominent Liberals.
10003{_}585{_}He said it was quite possible and then gave me various instructions.
10003{_}586{_}I was above all to make no fuss if they really came to arrest him.
10003{_}587{_}He showed me where all his keys, papers, and money were, told me to go instantly to his uncle, Mr. Lutteroth, who lived next door.
10003{_}588{_}He was an old diplomat, knew everybody, and would give me very good advice.
10003{_}589{_}I did not feel very happy, but like so many things that are foretold, nothing ever happened.
10003{_}590{_}Another rumour, from the extreme Left this time, was that a large armed force under the command of a well-known general, very high up in his career, was to assemble in the north at Lille, a strong contingent of Republicans were to join them to be ready to act.
10003{_}591{_}I remember quite well two of W.'s friends coming in one morning, full of enthusiasm for this plan.
10003{_}592{_}I don't think they quite knew what they were going to do with their army.
10003{_}593{_}W. certainly did not.
10003{_}594{_}He listened to all the details of the plan; they gave him the name of the general, supposed to have very Republican sympathies (not generally the case with officers), the number of regiments, etc., who would march at a given signal, but when he said, "It is possible, you might get a certain number of men together, but what would you do with them?"
10003{_}595{_}they were rather nonplussed.
10003{_}596{_}They hadn't got any further than a grand patriotic demonstration, with the military, drums beating, flags flying, and the Marseillaise being howled by an excited crowd.
10003{_}597{_}No such extreme measures, however, were ever carried out.
10003{_}598{_}From the first moment it was evident that a large Republican majority would be returned; almost all the former deputies were re-elected and a number of new ones, more advanced in their opinion.
10003{_}599{_}In the country it was the only topic of conversation.
10003{_}600{_}Parliament was dissolved in June, 1877, but we remained in town until the end of July.
10003{_}601{_}It wasn't very warm and many people remained until the end of the session.
10003{_}602{_}The big schools too only break up on the 15th of July, and many parents remain in Paris.
10003{_}603{_}The Republican campaign had already begun, and there were numerous little dinners and meetings when plans and possibilities were discussed.
10003{_}604{_}W. got back usually very late from Versailles.
10003{_}605{_}When he knew the sitting would be very late he sent me word and I used to go and dine with mother, but sometimes he was kept on there from hour to hour.
10003{_}606{_}I had some long waits before we could dine, and Hubert, the coachman, used to spend hours in the courtyard of the Gare St. Lazare waiting for his master.
10003{_}607{_}We had a big bay mare, a very fast trotter, which always did the train service, and the two were stationed there sometimes from six-thirty to nine-thirty, but they never seemed the worse for it.
10003{_}608{_}W., though a very considerate man for his servants generally, never worried at all about keeping his coachmen and horses waiting.
10003{_}609{_}He said the coachmen were the most warmly dressed men in Paris, always took care to be well covered, and we never had fancy, high-stepping horses, but ordinary strong ones, which could wait patiently.
10003{_}610{_}W. said the talk in the Chambers and in the lobbies was quite wild--every sort of extravagant proposition was made.
10003{_}611{_}There were many conferences with the Duc d'Audiffret-Pasquier, Duc de Broglie--with Casimir Perier, Leon Say, Gambetta, Jules Ferry, and Freycinet--where the best men on both sides tried hard to come to an agreement.
10003{_}612{_}W. went several times in August to see M. Thiers, who was settled at St. Germain.
10003{_}613{_}The old statesman was as keen as ever, receiving every day all sorts of deputations, advising, warning, encouraging, and quite confident as to the result of the elections.
10003{_}614{_}People were looking to him as the next President, despite his great age.
10003{_}615{_}However, he was not destined to see the triumph of his ideas.
10003{_}616{_}He died suddenly at St. Germain on the 3d of September.
10003{_}617{_}W. said his funeral was a remarkable sight--thousands of people followed the cortege--all Paris showing a last respect to the liberateur du territoire (though there were still clubs where he was spoken of as le sinistre vieillard).
10003{_}618{_}In August W. went to his Conseil-General at Laon, and I went down to my brother-in-law's place at St. Leger near Rouen.
10003{_}619{_}We were a very happy cosmopolitan family-party.
10003{_}620{_}My mother-in-law was born a Scotch-woman (Chisholm).
10003{_}621{_}She was a fine type of the old-fashioned cultivated lady, with a charming polite manner, keenly interested in all that was going on in the world.
10003{_}622{_}She was an old lady when I married, and had outlived almost all her contemporaries, but she had a beautiful old age, surrounded by children and grandchildren.
10003{_}623{_}She had lived through many vicissitudes from the time of her marriage, when she arrived at the Chateau of St. Remy in the Department of Eure-et-Loire (where my husband, her eldest son, was born), passing through triumphal arches erected in honour of the young bride, to the last days when the fortunes of the family were diminished by revolutions and political and business crises in France.
10003{_}624{_}They moved from St. Remy, selling the chateau, and built a house on the top of a green hill near Rouen, quite shut in by big trees, and with a lovely view from the Rond Point--the highest part of the garden, over Rouen--with the spires of the cathedral in the distance.
10003{_}625{_}I used to find her every morning when I went to her room, sitting at the window, her books and knitting on a table near--looking down on the lawn and the steep winding path that came up from the garden,--where she had seen three generations of her dear ones pass every day--first her husband, then her sons--now her grandsons.
10003{_}626{_}My sister-in-law, R.'s wife, was also an Englishwoman; the daughter of the house had married her cousin, de Bunsen, who had been a German diplomatist, and who had made nearly all his career in Italy, at the most interesting period of her history, when she was struggling for emancipation from the Austrian rule and independence.
10003{_}627{_}I was an American, quite a new element in the family circle.
10003{_}628{_}We had many and most animated discussions over all sorts of subjects, in two or three languages, at the tea-table under the big tree on the lawn.
10003{_}629{_}French and English were always going, and often German, as de Bunsen always spoke to his daughter in German.
10003{_}630{_}My mother-in-law, who knew three or four languages, did not at all approve of the careless habit we had all got into of mixing our languages and using French or Italian words when we were speaking English--if they came more easily.
10003{_}631{_}She made a rule that we should use only one language at meals--she didn't care which one, but we must keep to it.
10003{_}632{_}My brother-in-law was standing for the deputation.
10003{_}633{_}We didn't see much of him in the daytime--his electors and his visits and speeches and banquets de pompiers took up all his time.
10003{_}634{_}The beginning of his career had been very different.
10003{_}635{_}He was educated in England--Rugby and Woolwich--and served several years in the Royal Artillery in the British army.
10003{_}636{_}His military training was very useful to him during the Franco-Prussian War, when he equipped and commanded a field battery, making all the campaign.
10003{_}637{_}His English brother officers always remembered him.
10003{_}638{_}Many times when we were living in England at the embassy, I was asked about him.
10003{_}639{_}A curious thing happened in the House of Lords one day, showing the wonderful memory of princes for faces.
10003{_}640{_}R. was staying with us for a few days, when the annual debate over the bill for marriage of a deceased wife's sister came up.
10003{_}641{_}The Prince of Wales (late King Edward) and all the other princes were present in the House.
10003{_}642{_}R. was there too, standing where all the strangers do, at the entrance of the lobby.
10003{_}643{_}When the debate was over, the Prince of Wales left.
10003{_}644{_}As he passed along, he shook hands with several gentlemen also standing near the lobby, including R. He stopped a moment in front of him, saying: "I think this is Mr. Waddington.
10003{_}645{_}The last time I saw you, you wore Her Majesty's uniform."
10003{_}646{_}He hadn't seen him for twenty-five or thirty years.
10003{_}647{_}I asked the prince afterward how he recognised him.
10003{_}648{_}He said he didn't know; it was perhaps noticing an unfamiliar face in the group of men standing there,--and something recalled his brother, the ambassador.
10003{_}649{_}In September we went down to Bourneville and settled ourselves there for the autumn.
10003{_}650{_}W. was standing for the Senate with the Count de St. Vallier and Henri Martin.
10003{_}651{_}They all preferred being named in their department, where everybody knew them and their personal influence could make itself more easily felt.
10003{_}652{_}W.'s campaign was not very arduous.
10003{_}653{_}All the people knew him and liked him--knew that he would do whatever he promised.
10003{_}654{_}Their programme was absolutely Republican, but moderate, and he only made a few speeches and went about the country a little.
10003{_}655{_}I often went with him when he rode, and some of our visits to the farmers and local authorities were amusing if not encouraging.
10003{_}656{_}We were always very well received, but it wasn't easy to find out what they really thought (if they did think about it at all) of the state of affairs.
10003{_}657{_}The small landowners particularly, the men who had one field and a garden, were very reserved.
10003{_}658{_}They listened attentively enough to all W. had to say.
10003{_}659{_}He was never long, never personal, and never abused his adversaries, but they rarely expressed an opinion.
10003{_}660{_}They almost always turned the conversation upon some local matter or petty grievance.
10003{_}661{_}It didn't seem to me that they took the slightest interest in the extraordinary changes that were going on in France.
10003{_}662{_}A great many people came to see W. and there would be a curious collection sometimes in his library at the end of the day.
10003{_}663{_}The doctor (who always had precise information--country doctors always have--they see a great many people and I fancy the women talk to them and tell them what their men are doing), one or two farmers, some schoolmasters, the mayors of the nearest villages, the captains of the firemen and of the archers (they still shoot with bow and arrow in our part of the country; every Sunday the men practise shooting at a target)--the gendarmes, very useful these too to bring news--the notary, and occasionally a sous-prefet, but then he was a personage, representing the Government, and was treated with more ceremony than the other visitors.
10003{_}664{_}It was evident from all these sources that the Republicans were coming to the front en masse.
10003{_}665{_}The Republicans (for once) were marvellously disciplined and kept together.
10003{_}666{_}It was really wonderful when one thought of all the different elements that were represented in the party.
10003{_}667{_}There was quite as much difference between the quiet moderate men of the Left Centre and the extreme Left as there was between the Legitimists and any faction of the Republican party.
10003{_}668{_}There was a strong feeling among the Liberals that they were being coerced, that arbitrary measures, perhaps a coup d'etat, would be sprung upon them, and they were quite determined to resist.
10003{_}669{_}I don't think there was ever any danger of a coup d'etat, at least as long as Marshal MacMahon was the chief of state.
10003{_}670{_}He was a fine honourable, patriotic soldier, utterly incapable of an illegality of any kind.
10003{_}671{_}He didn't like the Republic, honestly thought it would never succeed with the Republicans (la Republique sans Republicains was for him its only chance)--and he certainly had illusions and thought his friends and advisers would succeed in making and keeping a firm conservative government.
10003{_}672{_}How far that illusion was shared by his entourage it is difficult to say.
10003{_}673{_}They fought their battle well--government pressure exercised in all ways.
10003{_}674{_}Prefets and sous-prefets changed, wonderful prospects of little work and high pay held out to doubtful electors, and the same bright illusive promises made to the masses, which all parties make in all elections and which the people believe each time.
10003{_}675{_}The Republicans were not idle either, and many fiery patriotic speeches were made or their side.
10003{_}676{_}Gambetta always held his public with his passionate, earnest declamation, and his famous phrase, that the marshal must "se soumettre ou se demettre," became a password all through the country.
10003{_}677{_}V   A REPUBLICAN VICTORY AND A NEW MINISTRY  The elections took place in October-November, 1877, and gave at once a great Republican majority.
10003{_}678{_}W. and his two colleagues, Count de St. Vallier and Henri Martin, had an easy victory, but a great many of their personal friends, moderates, were beaten.
10003{_}679{_}The centres were decidedly weaker in the new Chambers.
10003{_}680{_}There was not much hope left of uniting the two centres, Droite et Gauche, in the famous "fusion" which had been a dream of the moderate men.
10003{_}681{_}The new Chambers assembled at Versailles in November.
10003{_}682{_}The Broglie cabinet was out, but a new ministry of the Right faced the new Parliament.
10003{_}683{_}Their life was very short and stormy; they were really dead before they began to exist and in December the marshal sent for M. Dufaure and charged him to form a Ministere de Gauche.
10003{_}684{_}None of his personal friends, except General Borel at the War Office, was in the new combination.
10003{_}685{_}W. was named to the Foreign Office.
10003{_}686{_}I was rather disappointed when he came home and told me he had accepted that portfolio.
10003{_}687{_}I thought his old ministry, Public Instruction, suited him so well, the work interested him, was entirely to his taste.
10003{_}688{_}He knew all the literary and educational world, not only in France but everywhere else--England, of course, where he had kept up with many of his Cambridge comrades, and Germany, where he also had literary connections.
10003{_}689{_}However, that wide acquaintance and his perfect knowledge of English and English people helped him very much at once, not only at the Quai d'Orsay, but in all the years he was in England as ambassador.
10003{_}690{_}The new ministry, with Dufaure as President of the Council, Leon Say at the Finances, M. de Freycinet at Public Works, and W. at the Foreign Office was announced the 14th of December, 1877.
10003{_}691{_}The preliminaries had been long and difficult--the marshal and his friends on one side--the Republicans and Gambetta on the other--the moderates trying to keep things together.
10003{_}692{_}Personally, I was rather sorry W. had agreed to be a member of the cabinet; I was not very keen about official life and foresaw a great deal that would be disagreeable.
10003{_}693{_}Politics played such a part in social life.
10003{_}694{_}All the "society," the Faubourg St. Germain (which represents the old names and titles of France), was violently opposed to the Republic.
10003{_}695{_}I was astonished the first years of my married life in France, to see people of certain position and standing give the cold shoulder to men they had known all their lives because they were Republicans, knowing them quite well to be honourable, independent gentlemen, wanting nothing from the Republic--merely trying to do their best for the country.
10003{_}696{_}I only realised by degrees that people held off a little from me sometimes, as the wife of a Republican deputy.
10003{_}697{_}I didn't care particularly, as I had never lived in France, and knew very few people, but it didn't make social relations very pleasant, and I should have been better pleased if W. had taken no active part.
10003{_}698{_}However, that feeling was only temporary.
10003{_}699{_}I soon became keenly interested in politics (I suppose it is in the blood--all the men in my family in America were politicians) and in the discussion of the various questions which were rapidly changing France into something quite different.
10003{_}700{_}Whether the change has been for the better it would be hard to say even now, after more than thirty-five years of the Republic.
10003{_}701{_}Freycinet was a great strength.
10003{_}702{_}He was absolutely Republican, but moderate--very clever and energetic, a great friend of Gambetta's--and a beautiful speaker.
10003{_}703{_}I have heard men say who didn't care about him particularly, and who were not at all of his way of thinking, that they would rather not discuss with him.
10003{_}704{_}He was sure to win them over to his cause with his wonderful, clear persuasive arguments.
10003{_}705{_}[Illustration: Palace of the Minister of Foreign Affairs, Paris.]
10003{_}706{_}The first days were very busy ones.
10003{_}707{_}W. had to see all his staff (a very large one) of the Foreign Office, and organise his own cabinet.
10003{_}708{_}He was out all day, until late in the evening, at the Quai d'Orsay; used to go over there about ten or ten-thirty, breakfast there, and get back for a very late dinner, and always had a director or secretary working with him at our own house after dinner.
10003{_}709{_}I went over three or four times to inspect the ministry, as I had a presentiment we should end by living there.
10003{_}710{_}The house is large and handsome, with a fine staircase and large high rooms.
10003{_}711{_}The furniture of course was "ministerial"--stiff and heavy--gold-backed chairs and sofas standing in rows against the walls.
10003{_}712{_}There were some good pictures, among others the "Congres de Paris," which occupies a prominent place in one of the salons, and splendid tapestries.
10003{_}713{_}The most attractive thing was a fine large garden at the back, but, as the living-rooms were up-stairs, we didn't use it very much.
10003{_}714{_}The lower rooms, which opened on the gardens, were only used as reception-rooms.
10003{_}715{_}The minister's cabinet was also down-stairs, communicating by a small staircase with his bedroom, just overhead.
10003{_}716{_}The front of the house looks on the Seine; we had always a charming view from the windows, at night particularly, when all the little steamers (mouches) were passing with their lights.
10003{_}717{_}I had of course to make acquaintance with all the diplomatic corps.
10003{_}718{_}I knew all the ambassadors and most of the ministers, but there were some representatives of the smaller powers and South American Republics with whom I had never come in contact.
10003{_}719{_}Again I paid a formal official visit to the Marechale de MacMahon as soon as the ministry was announced.
10003{_}720{_}She was perfectly polite and correct, but one felt at once she hadn't the slightest sympathy for anything Republican, and we never got to know each other any better all the months we were thrown together.
10003{_}721{_}We remained for several weeks at our own house, and then most reluctantly determined to install ourselves at the ministry.
10003{_}722{_}W. worked always very late after dinner, and he felt it was not possible to ask his directors, all important men of a certain age, to come up to the Quartier de l'Etoile at ten o'clock and keep them busy until midnight.
10003{_}723{_}W.'s new chef de cabinet, Comte de Pontecoulant, was very anxious that we should move, thought everything would be simplified if W. were living over there.
10003{_}724{_}I had never known Pontecoulant until W. chose him as his chef de cabinet.
10003{_}725{_}He was a diplomatist with some years of service behind him, and was perfectly au courant of all the routine and habits of the Foreign Office.
10003{_}726{_}He paid me a short formal visit soon after he had accepted the post; we exchanged a few remarks about the situation, I hoped we would faire bon menage, and had no particular impression of him except that he was very French and stiff; I didn't suppose I should see much of him.
10003{_}727{_}It seems curious now to look back upon that first interview.
10003{_}728{_}We all became so fond of him, he was a loyal, faithful friend, was always ready to help me in any small difficulties, and I went to him for everything--visits, servants, horses, etc.
10003{_}729{_}W. had no time for any details or amenities of life.
10003{_}730{_}We moved over just before New Year's day.
10003{_}731{_}As the gros mobilier was already there, we only took over personal things, grand piano, screens, tables, easy chairs, and small ornaments and bibelots.
10003{_}732{_}These were all sent off in a van early one morning, and after luncheon I went over, having given rendezvous to Pontecoulant and M. Kruft, chef du materiel, an excellent, intelligent man, who was most useful and devoted to me the two years I lived at the ministry.
10003{_}733{_}I was very depressed when we drove into the courtyard.
10003{_}734{_}I had never lived on that side of the river, and felt cut off from all my belongings,--the bridge a terror, so cold in winter, so hot in summer,--I never got accustomed to it, never crossed it on foot.
10003{_}735{_}The sight of the great empty rooms didn't reassure me.
10003{_}736{_}The reception-rooms of course were very handsome.
10003{_}737{_}There were a great many servants, huissiers, and footmen standing about, and people waiting in the big drawing-room to speak to W. The living-rooms up-stairs were ghastly--looked bare and uncomfortable in the highest degree.
10003{_}738{_}They were large and high and looked down upon the garden, though that on a bleak December day was not very cheerful--but there were possibilities.
10003{_}739{_}Kruft was very sympathetic, understood quite well how I felt, and was ready to do anything in the way of stoves, baths, wardrobes in the lingerie, new carpets, and curtains, that I wanted.
10003{_}740{_}Pontecoulant too was eminently practical, and I was quite amused to find myself discussing lingeries and bathrooms with a total stranger whom I had only seen twice in my life.
10003{_}741{_}It took me about a week to get really settled.
10003{_}742{_}I went over every day, returning to my own house to eat and sleep.
10003{_}743{_}Kruft did wonders; the place was quite transformed when I finally moved over.
10003{_}744{_}The rooms looked very bright and comfortable when we arrived in the afternoon of the 31st of December (New Year's eve).
10003{_}745{_}The little end salon, which I made my boudoir, was hung with blue satin; my piano, screens, and little things were very well placed--plenty of palms and flowers, bright fires everywhere--the bedrooms, nursery, and lingeries clean and bright.
10003{_}746{_}My bedroom opened on a large salon, where I received usually, keeping my boudoir for ourselves and our intimate friends.
10003{_}747{_}My special huissier, Gerard, who sat all day outside of the salon door, was presented to me, and instantly became a most useful and important member of the household--never forgot a name or a face, remembered what cards and notes I had received, whether the notes were answered, or the bills paid, knew almost all my wardrobe, would bring me down a coat or a wrap if I wanted one suddenly down-stairs.
10003{_}748{_}I had frequent consultations with Pontecoulant and Kruft to regulate all the details of the various services before we were quite settled.
10003{_}749{_}We took over all our own servants and found many others who were on the permanent staff of the ministry, footmen, huissiers, and odd men who attended to all the fires, opened and shut all the doors, windows, and shutters.
10003{_}750{_}It was rather difficult to organise the regular working service, there was such rivalry between our own personal servants and the men who belonged to the house, but after a little while things went pretty smoothly.
10003{_}751{_}W. dined out the first night we slept at the Quai d'Orsay, and about an hour after we had arrived, while I was still walking about in my hat and coat, feeling very strange in the big, high rooms, I was told that the lampiste was waiting my orders (a few lamps had been lit in some of the rooms).
10003{_}752{_}I didn't quite know what orders to give, hadn't mastered yet the number that would be required; but I sent for him, said I should be alone for dinner, perhaps one or two lamps in the dining-room and small salon would be enough.
10003{_}753{_}He evidently thought that was not at all sufficient, wanted something more precise, so I said to light as he had been accustomed to when the Duc Decazes and his family were dining alone (which I don't suppose they ever did, nor we either when we once took up our life).
10003{_}754{_}Such a blaze of light met my eyes when I went to dinner that I was quite bewildered--boudoir, billiard-room, dining-room (very large, the small round table for one person hardly perceptible), and corridors all lighted "a giorno."
10003{_}755{_}However, it looked very cheerful and kept me from feeling too dreadfully homesick for my own house and familiar surroundings.
10003{_}756{_}The rooms were so high up that we didn't hear the noise of the street, but the river looked alive and friendly with the lights on the bridges, and a few boats still running.
10003{_}757{_}We had much more receiving and entertaining to do at the Quai d'Orsay than at any other ministry, and were obliged to go out much more ourselves.
10003{_}758{_}The season in the official world begins with a reception at the President's on New Year's day.
10003{_}759{_}The diplomatic corps and presidents of the Senate and Chamber go in state to the Elysee to pay their respects to the chief of state--the ambassadors with all their staff in uniform in gala carriages.
10003{_}760{_}It is a pretty sight, and there are always a good many people waiting in the Faubourg St. Honore to see the carriages.
10003{_}761{_}The English carriage is always the best; they understand all the details of harness and livery so much better than any one else.
10003{_}762{_}The marshal and his family were established at the Elysee.
10003{_}763{_}It wasn't possible for him to remain at Versailles--he couldn't be so far from Paris, where all sorts of questions were coming up every day, and he was obliged to receive deputations and reports, and see people of all kinds.
10003{_}764{_}They were already agitating the question of the Parliament coming back to Paris.
10003{_}765{_}The deputies generally were complaining of the loss of time and the discomfort of the daily journey even in the parliamentary train.
10003{_}766{_}The Right generally was very much opposed to having the Chambers back in Paris.
10003{_}767{_}I never could understand why.
10003{_}768{_}I suppose they were afraid that a stormy sitting might lead to disturbances.
10003{_}769{_}In the streets of a big city there is always a floating population ready to espouse violently any cause.
10003{_}770{_}At Versailles one was away from any such danger, and, except immediately around the palace, there was nobody in the long, deserted avenues.
10003{_}771{_}They often cited the United States, how no statesman after the signing of the Declaration of Independence (in Philadelphia) would have ventured to propose that the Parliament should sit in New York or Philadelphia, but the reason there was very different; they were obliged to make a neutral zone, something between the North and the South.
10003{_}772{_}The District of Columbia is a thing apart, belonging to neither side.
10003{_}773{_}It has certainly worked very well in America.
10003{_}774{_}Washington is a fine city, with its splendid old trees and broad avenues.
10003{_}775{_}It has a cachet of its own, is unlike any other city I know in the world.
10003{_}776{_}The marshal received at the Elysee every Thursday evening--he and his staff in uniform, also all the officers who came, which made a brilliant gathering.
10003{_}777{_}Their big dinners and receptions were always extremely well done.
10003{_}778{_}Except a few of their personal friends, not many people of society were present--the diplomatic corps usually very well represented, the Government and their wives, and a certain number of liberal deputies--a great many officers.
10003{_}779{_}We received every fifteen days, beginning with a big dinner.
10003{_}780{_}It was an open reception, announced in the papers.
10003{_}781{_}The diplomats always mustered very strong, also the Parliament--not many women.
10003{_}782{_}Many of the deputies remained in the country, taking rooms merely while the Chambers were sitting, and their wives never appeared in Paris.
10003{_}783{_}"Society" didn't come to us much either, except on certain occasions when we had a royal prince or some very distinguished foreigners.
10003{_}784{_}Besides the big official receptions, we often had small dinners up-stairs during the week.
10003{_}785{_}Some of these I look back to with much pleasure.
10003{_}786{_}I was generally the only lady with eight or ten men, and the talk was often brilliant.
10003{_}787{_}Some of our habitues were the late Lord Houghton, a delightful talker; Lord Dufferin, then ambassador in St. Petersburg; Sir Henry Layard, British ambassador in Spain, an interesting man who had been everywhere and seen and known everybody worth knowing in the world; Count Schouvaloff, Russian ambassador in London, a polished courtier, extremely intelligent; he and W. were colleagues afterward at the Congres de Berlin, and W. has often told me how brilliantly he defended his cause; General Ignatieff, Prince Orloff, the nunzio Monsignor Czascki, quite charming, the type of the prelat mondain, very large (though very Catholic) in his ideas, but never aggressive or disagreeable about the Republic, as so many of the clergy were.
10003{_}788{_}He was very fond of music, and went with me sometimes to the Conservatoire on Sunday; he had a great admiration for the way they played classical music; used to lean back in his chair in a corner (would never sit in front of the box) and drink in every sound.
10003{_}789{_}We sometimes had informal music in my little blue salon.
10003{_}790{_}Baron de Zuylen, Dutch minister, was an excellent musician, also Comte de Beust, the Austrian ambassador.
10003{_}791{_}He was a composer.
10003{_}792{_}I remember his playing me one day a wedding march he had composed for the marriage of one of the archdukes.
10003{_}793{_}It was very descriptive, with bells, cannon, hurrahs, and a nuptial hymn--rather difficult to render on a piano--but there was a certain amount of imagination in the composition.
10003{_}794{_}The two came often with me to the Conservatoire.
10003{_}795{_}Comte de Beust brought Liszt to me one day.
10003{_}796{_}I wanted so much to see that complex character, made up of enthusiasms of all kinds, patriotic, religious, musical.
10003{_}797{_}He was dressed in the ordinary black priestly garb, looked like an ascetic with pale, thin face, which lighted up very much when discussing any subject that interested him.
10003{_}798{_}He didn't say a word about music, either then or on a subsequent occasion when I lunched with him at the house of a great friend and admirer, who was a beautiful musician.
10003{_}799{_}I hoped he would play after luncheon.
10003{_}800{_}He was a very old man, and played rarely in those days, but one would have liked to hear him.
10003{_}801{_}Madame M. thought he would perhaps for her, if the party were not too large, and the guests "sympathetic" to him.
10003{_}802{_}I have heard so many artists say it made all the difference to them when they felt the public was with them--if there were one unsympathetic or criticising face in the mass of people, it was the only face they could distinguish, and it affected them very much.
10003{_}803{_}The piano was engagingly open and music littered about, but he apparently didn't see it.
10003{_}804{_}He talked politics, and a good deal about pictures with some artists who were present.
10003{_}805{_}[Illustration: Franz Liszt.]
10003{_}806{_}I did hear him play many years later in London.
10003{_}807{_}We were again lunching together, at the house of a mutual friend, who was not at all musical.
10003{_}808{_}There wasn't even a piano in the house, but she had one brought in for the occasion.
10003{_}809{_}When I arrived rather early, the day of the party, I found the mistress of the house, aided by Count Hatzfeldt, then German ambassador to England, busily engaged in transforming her drawing-room.
10003{_}810{_}The grand piano, which had been standing well out toward the middle of the room, open, with music on it (I dare say some of Liszt's own--but I didn't have time to examine), was being pushed back into a corner, all the music hidden away, and the instrument covered with photographs, vases of flowers, statuettes, heavy books, all the things one doesn't habitually put on pianos.
10003{_}811{_}I was quite puzzled, but Hatzfeldt, who was a great friend of Liszt's and knew all his peculiarities, when consulted by Madame A. as to what she could do to induce Liszt to play, had answered: "Begin by putting the piano in the furthest, darkest corner of the room, and put all sorts of heavy things on it.
10003{_}812{_}Then he won't think you have asked him in the hope of hearing him play, and perhaps we can persuade him."
10003{_}813{_}The arrangements were just finished as the rest of the company arrived.
10003{_}814{_}We were not a large party, and the talk was pleasant enough.
10003{_}815{_}Liszt looked much older, so colourless, his skin like ivory, but he seemed just as animated and interested in everything.
10003{_}816{_}After luncheon, when they were smoking (all of us together, no one went into the smoking-room), he and Hatzfeldt began talking about the Empire and the beautiful fetes at Compiegne, where anybody of any distinction in any branch of art or literature was invited.
10003{_}817{_}Hatzfeldt led the conversation to some evenings when Strauss played his waltzes with an entrain, a sentiment that no one else has ever attained, and to Offenbach and his melodies--one evening particularly when he had improvised a song for the Empress--he couldn't quite remember it.
10003{_}818{_}If there were a piano--he looked about.
10003{_}819{_}There was none apparently.
10003{_}820{_}"Oh, yes, in a corner, but so many things upon it, it was evidently never meant to be opened."
10003{_}821{_}He moved toward it, Liszt following, asking Comtesse A. if it could be opened.
10003{_}822{_}The things were quickly removed.
10003{_}823{_}Hatzfeldt sat down and played a few bars in rather a halting fashion.
10003{_}824{_}After a moment Liszt said: "No, no, it is not quite that."
10003{_}825{_}Hatzfeldt got up.
10003{_}826{_}Liszt seated himself at the piano, played two or three bits of songs, or waltzes, then, always talking to Hatzfeldt, let his fingers wander over the keys and by degrees broke into a nocturne and a wild Hungarian march.
10003{_}827{_}It was very curious; his fingers looked as if they were made of yellow ivory, so thin and long, and of course there wasn't any strength or execution in his playing--it was the touch of an old man, but a master--quite unlike anything I have ever heard.
10003{_}828{_}When he got up, he said: "Oh, well, I didn't think the old fingers had any music left in them."
10003{_}829{_}We tried to thank him, but he wouldn't listen to us, immediately talked about something else.
10003{_}830{_}When he had gone we complimented the ambassador on the way in which he had managed the thing.
10003{_}831{_}Hatzfeldt was a charming colleague, very clever, very musical, a thorough man of the world.
10003{_}832{_}I was always pleased when he was next to me at dinner--I was sure of a pleasant hour.
10003{_}833{_}He had been many years in Paris during the brilliant days of the Empire, knew everybody there worth knowing.
10003{_}834{_}He had the reputation, notwithstanding his long stay in Paris, of being very anti-French.
10003{_}835{_}I could hardly judge of that, as he never talked politics to me.
10003{_}836{_}It may very likely have been true, but not more marked with him than with the generality of Anglo-Saxons and Northern races, who rather look down upon the Latins, hardly giving them credit for their splendid dash and pluck--to say nothing of their brains.
10003{_}837{_}I have lived in a great many countries, and always think that as a people, I mean the uneducated mass, the French are the most intelligent nation in the world.
10003{_}838{_}I have never been thrown with the Japanese--am told they are extraordinarily intelligent.
10003{_}839{_}We had a dinner one night for Mr. Gladstone, his wife, and a daughter.
10003{_}840{_}Mr. Gladstone made himself quite charming, spoke French fairly well, and knew more about every subject discussed than any one else in the room.
10003{_}841{_}He was certainly a wonderful man, such extraordinary versatility and such a memory.
10003{_}842{_}It was rather pretty to see Mrs. Gladstone when her husband was talking.
10003{_}843{_}She was quite absorbed by him, couldn't talk to her neighbours.
10003{_}844{_}They wanted very much to go to the Conciergerie to see the prison where the unfortunate Marie Antoinette passed the last days of her unhappy life, and Mr. Gladstone, inspired by the subject, made us a sort of conference on the French Revolution and the causes which led up to it, culminating in the Terror and the execution of the King and Queen.
10003{_}845{_}He spoke in English (we were a little group standing at the door--they were just going), in beautiful academic language, and it was most interesting, graphic, and exact.
10003{_}846{_}Even W., who knew him well and admired him immensely, was struck by his brilliant improvisation.
10003{_}847{_}[Illustration: William E. Gladstone.
10003{_}848{_}From a photograph by Samuel A. Walker, London.]
10003{_}849{_}We were often asked for permits by our English and American friends to see all the places of historical interest in Paris, and the two places which all wanted to see were the Conciergerie and Napoleon's tomb at the Invalides.
10003{_}850{_}When we first came to Paris in 1866, just after the end of the long struggle between the North and South in America, our first visits too were for the Conciergerie, Invalides, and Notre Dame, where my father had not been since he had gone as a very young man with all Paris to see the flags that had been brought back from Austerlitz.
10003{_}851{_}They were interesting days, those first ones in Paris, so full of memories for father, who had been there a great deal in his young days, first as an eleve in the Ecole Polytechnique, later when the Allies were in Paris.
10003{_}852{_}He took us one day to the Luxembourg Gardens, to see if he could find any trace of the spot where in 1815 during the Restoration Marshal Ney had been shot.
10003{_}853{_}He was in Paris at the time, and was in the garden a few hours after the execution--remembered quite well the wall against which the marshal stood--and the comments of the crowd, not very flattering for the Government in executing one of France's bravest and most brilliant soldiers.
10003{_}854{_}All the Americans who came to see us at the Quai d'Orsay were much interested in everything relating to General Marquis de Lafayette, who left an undying memory in America, and many pilgrimages were made to the Chateau de la Grange, where the Marquis de Lafayette spent the last years of his life and extended a large and gracious hospitality to all his friends.
10003{_}855{_}It is an interesting old place, with a moat all around it and high solid stone walls, where one still sees the hole that was made in the wall by a cannon-ball sent by Marechal de Turenne as he was passing with his troops, as a friendly souvenir to the owner, with whom he was not on good terms.
10003{_}856{_}So many Americans and English too are imbued with the idea that there are no chateaux, no country life in France, that I am delighted when they can see that there are just as many as in any other country.
10003{_}857{_}A very clever American writer, whose books have been much read and admired, says that when travelling in France in the country, he never saw any signs of wealth or gentlemen's property.
10003{_}858{_}I think he didn't want to admire anything French, but I wonder in what part of France he has travelled.
10003{_}859{_}Besides the well-known historic chateaux of Chaumont, Chenonceaux, Azay-le-Rideau, Maintenon, Dampierre, Josselin, Valencay, and scores of others, there are quantities of small Louis XV chateaux and manoirs, half hidden in a corner of a forest, which the stranger never sees.
10003{_}860{_}They are quite charming, built of red brick with white copings, with stiff old-fashioned gardens, and trees cut into all sorts of fantastic shapes.
10003{_}861{_}Sometimes the parish church touches the castle on one side, and there is a private entrance for the seigneurs.
10003{_}862{_}The interior arrangements in some of the old ones leave much to be desired in the way of comfort and modern improvements,--lighting very bad, neither gas nor electricity, and I should think no baths anywhere, hardly a tub.
10003{_}863{_}On the banks of the Seine and the Loire, near the great forests, in all the departments near Paris there are quantities of chateaux--some just on the border of the highroad, separated from it by high iron gates, through which one sees long winding alleys with stone benches and vases with red geraniums planted in them, a sun-dial and stiff formal rows of trees--some less pretentious with merely an ordinary wooden gate, generally open, and always flowers of the simplest kind, geraniums, sunflowers, pinks, dahlias, and chrysanthemums--what we call a jardin de cure, (curate's garden)--but in great abundance.
10003{_}864{_}With very rare exceptions the lawns are not well kept--one never sees in this country the smooth green turf that one does in England.
10003{_}865{_}Some of the old chateaux are very stately--sometimes one enters by a large quadrangle, quite surrounded by low arcades covered with ivy, a fountain and good-sized basin in the middle of the courtyard, and a big clock over the door--sometimes they stand in a moat, one goes over a drawbridge with massive doors, studded with iron nails and strong iron bolts and chains which defend the entrance, making one think of old feudal days, when might was right, and if a man wanted his neighbours property, he simply took it.
10003{_}866{_}Even some of the smaller chateaux have moats.
10003{_}867{_}I think they are more picturesque than comfortable--an ivy-covered house with a moat around it is a nest for mosquitoes and insects of all kinds, and I fancy the damp from the water must finish by pervading the house.
10003{_}868{_}French people of all classes love the country and a garden with bright flowers, and if the poorer ones can combine a rabbit hutch with the flowers they are quite happy.
10003{_}869{_}I have heard W. speak sometimes of a fine old chateau in our department--(Aisne) belonging to a deputy, who invited his friends to shoot and breakfast.
10003{_}870{_}The cuisine and shooting were excellent, but the accommodations fantastic.
10003{_}871{_}The neighbours said nothing had been renewed or cleaned since the chateau was occupied by the Cossacks under the first Napoleon.
10003{_}872{_}We got very little country life during those years at the Foreign Office.
10003{_}873{_}Twice a year, in April and August, W. went to Laon for his Conseil-General, over which he presided, but he was rarely able to stay all through the session.
10003{_}874{_}He was always present on the opening day, and at the prefet's dinner, and took that opportunity to make a short speech, explaining the foreign policy of the Government.
10003{_}875{_}I don't think it interested his colleagues as much as all the local questions--roads, schools, etc.
10003{_}876{_}It is astonishing how much time is wasted and how much letter-writing is necessitated by the simplest change in a road or railway crossing in France.
10003{_}877{_}We had rather a short narrow turning to get into our gate at Bourneville, and W. wanted to have the road enlarged just a little, so as to avoid the sharp angle.
10003{_}878{_}It didn't interfere with any one, as we were several yards from the highroad, but it was months, more than a year, before the thing was done.
10003{_}879{_}Any one of the workmen on the farm would have finished it in a day's work.
10003{_}880{_}At one of our small dinners I had such a characteristic answer from an English diplomatist, who had been ambassador at St. Petersburg.
10003{_}881{_}He was really a charming talker, but wouldn't speak French.
10003{_}882{_}That was of no consequence as long as he only talked to me, but naturally all the people at the table wanted to talk to him, and when the general conversation languished, at last, I said to him: "I wish you would speak French; none of these gentlemen speak any other language."
10003{_}883{_}(It was quite true, the men of my husband's age spoke very rarely any other language but their own; now almost all the younger generation speak German or English or both.
10003{_}884{_}Almost all my son's friends speak English perfectly.)
10003{_}885{_}"Oh no, I can't," he said; "I haven't enough the habit of speaking French.
10003{_}886{_}I don't say the things I want to say, only the things I can say, which is very different."
10003{_}887{_}"But what did you do in Russia?"
10003{_}888{_}"All the women speak English."
10003{_}889{_}"But for affairs, diplomatic negotiations?"
10003{_}890{_}"All the women speak English."
10003{_}891{_}I have often heard it said that the Russian women were much more clever than the men.
10003{_}892{_}He evidently had found it true.
10003{_}893{_}VI   THE EXPOSITION YEAR  The big political dinners were always interesting.
10003{_}894{_}On one occasion we had a banquet on the 2d of December.
10003{_}895{_}My left-hand neighbour, a senator, said to me casually: "This room looks very different from what it did the last time I was in it."
10003{_}896{_}"Does it?
10003{_}897{_}I should have thought a big official dinner at the Foreign Office would have been precisely the same under any regime."
10003{_}898{_}"A dinner perhaps, but on that occasion we were not precisely dining.
10003{_}899{_}I and a number of my friends had just been arrested, and we were waiting here in this room strictly guarded, until it was decided what should be done with us."
10003{_}900{_}Then I remembered that it was the 2d of December, the anniversary of Louis Napoleon's coup d'etat.
10003{_}901{_}He said they were quite unprepared for it, in spite of warnings.
10003{_}902{_}He was sent out of the country for a little while, but I don't think his exile was a very terrible one.
10003{_}903{_}I got my first lesson in diplomatic politeness from Lord Lyons, then British ambassador in Paris.
10003{_}904{_}He was in Paris during the Franco-German War, knew everybody, and had a great position.
10003{_}905{_}He gave very handsome dinners, liked his guests to be punctual, was very punctual himself, always arrived on the stroke of eight when he dined with us.
10003{_}906{_}We had an Annamite mission to dine one night and had invited almost all the ambassadors and ministers to meet them.
10003{_}907{_}There had been a stormy sitting at the Chamber and W. was late.
10003{_}908{_}As soon as I was ready I went to his library and waited for him; I couldn't go down and receive a foreign mission without him.
10003{_}909{_}We were quite seven or eight minutes late and found all the company assembled (except the Annamites, who were waiting with their interpreter in another room to make their entry in proper style).
10003{_}910{_}As I shook hands with Lord Lyons (who was doyen of the diplomatic corps) he said to me: "Ah, Madame Waddington, I see the Republic is becoming very royal; you don't receive your guests any more, merely come into the room when all the company is assembled."
10003{_}911{_}He said it quite smilingly, but I understood very well, and of course we ought to have been there when the first guests arrived.
10003{_}912{_}He was very amiable all the same and told me a great many useful things--for instance, that I must never invite a cardinal and an ambassador together, as neither of them would yield the precedence and I would find myself in a very awkward position.
10003{_}913{_}[Illustration: Lord Lyons.]
10003{_}914{_}The Annamites were something awful to see.
10003{_}915{_}In their country all the men of a certain standing blacken their teeth, and I suppose the dye makes their teeth fall out, as they hadn't any apparently, and when they opened their mouths the black caverns one saw were terrifying.
10003{_}916{_}I had been warned, but notwithstanding it made a most disagreeable impression on me.
10003{_}917{_}They were very richly attired, particularly the first three, who were tres grands seigneurs in Annam,--heavily embroidered silk robes, feathers, and jewels, and when they didn't open their mouths they were rather a decorative group,--were tall, powerfully built men.
10003{_}918{_}They knew no French nor English--spoke through the interpreter.
10003{_}919{_}My intercourse with them was very limited.
10003{_}920{_}They were not near me at dinner, but afterward I tried to talk to them a little.
10003{_}921{_}They all stood in a group at one end of the room, flanked by an interpreter--the three principal chiefs well in front.
10003{_}922{_}I don't know what the interpreter said to them from me, probably embellished my very banal remarks with flowers of rhetoric, but they were very smiling, opening wide their black mouths and made me very low bows--evidently appreciated my intention and effort to be amiable.
10003{_}923{_}They brought us presents, carpets, carved and inlaid mother-of-pearl boxes, cabinets, and some curious saddles, also gold-embroidered cushions and slippers.
10003{_}924{_}Some Arab horses were announced with great pomp from the Sultan's stables.
10003{_}925{_}I was rather interested in them, thought it would be amusing to drive a long-tailed Arab pony in a little cart in the morning.
10003{_}926{_}They were brought one morning to the Quai d'Orsay, and W. gave rendezvous to Comte de Pontecoulant and some of the sporting men of the cabinet, in the courtyard.
10003{_}927{_}There were also several stablemen, all much interested in the idea of taming the fiery steeds of the desert.
10003{_}928{_}The first look was disappointing.
10003{_}929{_}They were thin, scraggy animals, apparently all legs and manes.
10003{_}930{_}Long tails they had, and small heads, but anything so tame and sluggish in their movements could hardly be imagined.
10003{_}931{_}One could scarcely get them to canter around the courtyard.
10003{_}932{_}We were all rather disgusted, as sometimes one sees pretty little Arab horses in Paris.
10003{_}933{_}I don't know what became of them; I fancy they were sent to the cavalry stables.
10003{_}934{_}Our first great function that winter was the service at the Madeleine for the King of Italy, Victor Emmanuel, who died suddenly in the beginning of January, 1878.
10003{_}935{_}France sent a special mission to the funeral--the old Marshal Canrobert, who took with him the marshal's son, Fabrice de MacMahon.
10003{_}936{_}The Church of the Madeleine was filled with people of all kinds--the diplomatic corps in uniform, a very large representation of senators and deputies.
10003{_}937{_}There was a slight hesitation among some of the Left--who were ardent sympathisers with young Italy--but who didn't care to compromise themselves by taking part in a religious ceremony.
10003{_}938{_}However, as a rule they went.
10003{_}939{_}Some of the ladies of the Right were rather put out at having to go in deep mourning to the service.
10003{_}940{_}I said to one of them: "But you are not correct; you have a black dress certainly, but I don't think pearl-grey gloves are proper for such an occasion."
10003{_}941{_}"Oh, they express quite sufficiently the grief I feel on this occasion."
10003{_}942{_}It was curious that the King should have gone before the old Pope, who had been failing for some time.
10003{_}943{_}Every day we expected to hear of his death.
10003{_}944{_}There were many speculations over the new King of Italy, the Prince Humbert of our day.
10003{_}945{_}As we had lived so many years in Rome, I was often asked what he was like, but I really had no opinion.
10003{_}946{_}One saw him very little.
10003{_}947{_}I remember one day in the hunting-field he got a nasty fall.
10003{_}948{_}His horse put his foot in a hole and fell with him.
10003{_}949{_}It looked a bad accident, as if the horse were going to roll over on him.
10003{_}950{_}I, with one of my friends, was near, and seeing an accident (I didn't know who it was) naturally stopped to see if our groom could do anything, but an officer rode hurriedly up and begged us to go on, that the Prince would be very much annoyed if any one, particularly a woman, should notice his fall.
10003{_}951{_}I saw him later in the day, looking all right on another horse, and no one made any allusion to the accident.
10003{_}952{_}About a month after Victor Emmanuel's death the old Pope died, the 8th of February, 1878, quite suddenly at the end.
10003{_}953{_}He was buried of course in Rome, and it was very difficult to arrange for his funeral in the Rome of the King of Italy.
10003{_}954{_}However, he did lie in state at St. Peter's, the noble garde in their splendid uniforms standing close around the catafalque--long lines of Italian soldiers, the bersaglieri with their waving plumes, on each side of the great aisle.
10003{_}955{_}There was a magnificent service for him at Notre Dame.
10003{_}956{_}The Chambers raised their sitting as a mark of respect to the head of the church, and again there was a great attendance at the cathedral.
10003{_}957{_}There were many discussions in the monde (society not official) "as to whether one should wear mourning for the Saint Pere."
10003{_}958{_}I believe the correct thing is not to wear mourning, but almost all the ladies of the Faubourg St. Germain went about in black garments for some time.
10003{_}959{_}One of my friends put it rather graphically: "Si on a un ruban rose dans les cheveux on a tout de suite l'air d'etre la maitresse de Rochefort."
10003{_}960{_}All Europe was engrossed with the question of the Pope's successor.
10003{_}961{_}Intrigues and undercurrents were going on hard in Rome, and the issue of the conclave was impatiently awaited.
10003{_}962{_}No one could predict any result.
10003{_}963{_}The election of Cardinal Pecci, future Leo XIII, seemed satisfactory, at least in the beginning.
10003{_}964{_}My winter passed pleasantly enough; I began to feel more at home in my new quarters, and saw many interesting people of all kinds.
10003{_}965{_}Every now and then there would be a very lively debate in the Parliament.
10003{_}966{_}W. would come home very late, saying things couldn't go on like that, and we would surely be out of office in a few weeks.
10003{_}967{_}We always kept our house in the rue Dumont d'Urville, and I went over every week, often thinking that in a few days we should be back there again.
10003{_}968{_}One of my great trials was a reception day.
10003{_}969{_}W. thought I ought to have one, so every Friday I was at home from three until six, and very long afternoons they were.
10003{_}970{_}I insisted upon having a tea-table, which was a novelty in those days, but it broke the stiff semicircle of red and gold armchairs carefully arranged at one end of the room.
10003{_}971{_}Very few men took tea.
10003{_}972{_}It was rather amusing to see some of the deputies who didn't exactly like to refuse a cup of tea offered to them by the minister's wife, holding the cup and saucer most carefully in their hands, making a pretence of sipping the tea and replacing it hastily on the table as soon as it was possible.
10003{_}973{_}I had of course a great many people of different nationalities, who generally didn't know each other.
10003{_}974{_}The ambassadresses and ministers' wives sat on each side of my sofa--the smaller people lower down.
10003{_}975{_}They were all announced, my huissier, Gerard, doing it very well, opening the big doors and roaring out the names.
10003{_}976{_}Sometimes, at the end of the day, some of my own friends or some of the young men from the chancery would come in, and that would cheer me up a little.
10003{_}977{_}There was no conversation, merely an exchange of formal phrases, but I had some funny experiences.
10003{_}978{_}One day I had several ladies whom I didn't know at all, wives of deputies, or small functionaries at some of the ministries.
10003{_}979{_}One of my friends, Comtesse de B., was starting for Italy and Rome for the first time.
10003{_}980{_}She had come to ask me all sorts of questions about clothes, hotels, people to see, etc.
10003{_}981{_}When she went away in a whirl of preparations and addresses, I turned to one of my neighbours, saying: "Je crois qu'on est tres bien a l'Hotel de Londres a Rome," quite an insignificant and inoffensive remark--merely to say something.
10003{_}982{_}She replied haughtily: "Je n'en sais rien, Madame; je n'ai jamais quitte Paris et je m'en vante."
10003{_}983{_}I was so astonished that I had nothing to say, but was afterward sorry that I had not continued the conversation and asked her why she was so especially proud of never having left Paris.
10003{_}984{_}Travelling is usually supposed to enlarge one's ideas.
10003{_}985{_}Her answer might have been interesting.
10003{_}986{_}W. wouldn't believe it when I told him, but I said I couldn't really have invented it.
10003{_}987{_}I used to go into his cabinet at the end of the day always, when he was alone with Pontecoulant, and tell them all my experiences which W. forbid me to mention anywhere else.
10003{_}988{_}I had a good many surprises, but soon learned never to be astonished and to take everything as a matter of course.
10003{_}989{_}The great interest of the summer was the Exposition Universelle which was to take place at the Trocadero, the new building which had been built on the Champ de Mars.
10003{_}990{_}The opening was announced for the 1st of May and was to be performed with great pomp by the marshal.
10003{_}991{_}All Europe was represented except Germany, and almost all the great powers were sending princes to represent their country.
10003{_}992{_}We went often to see how the works were getting on, and I must say it didn't look as if it could possibly be ready for the 1st of May.
10003{_}993{_}There were armies of workmen in every direction and carts and camions loaded with cases making their way with difficulty through the mud.
10003{_}994{_}Occasionally a light case or bale would fall off, and quantities of small boys who seemed always on the spot would precipitate themselves, tumbling over each other to pick up what fell, and there would be protestations and explanations in every language under the sun.
10003{_}995{_}It was a motley, picturesque crowd--the costumes and uniforms making so much colour in the midst of the very ordinary dark clothes the civilised Western world affects.
10003{_}996{_}I felt sorry for the Orientals and people from milder climes--they looked so miserably cold and wretched shivering under the very fresh April breezes that swept over the great plain of the Champ de Mars.
10003{_}997{_}The machines, particularly the American ones, attracted great attention.
10003{_}998{_}There was always a crowd waiting when some of the large pieces were swung down into their places by enormous pulleys.
10003{_}999{_}The opening ceremony was very brilliant.
10003{_}1000{_}Happily it was a beautiful warm day, as all the guests invited by the marshal and the Government were seated on a platform outside the Trocadero building.
10003{_}1001{_}All the diplomatic corps, foreign royalties, and commissioners of the different nations who were taking part in the exposition were invited.
10003{_}1002{_}The view was lovely as we looked down from our seats.
10003{_}1003{_}The great enclosure was packed with people.
10003{_}1004{_}All the pavilions looked very gay with bright-coloured walls and turrets, and there were flags, palms, flowers, and fountains everywhere--the Seine running through the middle with fanciful bridges and boats.
10003{_}1005{_}There was a curious collection of people in the tribunes.
10003{_}1006{_}The invitations had not been very easy to make.
10003{_}1007{_}There were three Spanish sovereigns, Queen Isabella, her husband, Don Francois d'Assizes, and the Duc d'Aosta (King Amadee), who had reigned a few stormy months in Spain.
10003{_}1008{_}He had come to represent Italy at the exposition.
10003{_}1009{_}The marshal was rather preoccupied with his Spanish royalties.
10003{_}1010{_}He had a reception in the evening, to which all were invited, and thought it would be wise to take certain precautions, so he sent one of his aides-de-camp to Queen Isabella to say that he hoped to have the honour of seeing her in the evening at the Elysee, but he thought it right to tell her that she might perhaps have some disagreeable meetings.
10003{_}1011{_}She replied: "Si c'est mon mari de qui vous parlez, cela m'est tout a fait egal; si c'est le Duc d'Aosta, je serai ravie de le voir."
10003{_}1012{_}She came to the reception, but her husband was already gone.
10003{_}1013{_}The Due d'Aosta was still there, and she walked straight up to him and kissed him on both cheeks, not an easy thing to do, for the duke was not at all the type of the gay lady's man--very much the reverse.
10003{_}1014{_}He looked a soldier (like all the princes of the house of Savoy) and at the same time a monk.
10003{_}1015{_}One could easily imagine him a crusader in plumed helmet and breastplate, supporting any privation or fatigue without a murmur.
10003{_}1016{_}He was very shy (one saw it was an effort for him every time that any one was brought up to him and he had to make polite phrases), not in the least mondain, but simple, charming when one talked to him.
10003{_}1017{_}I saw him often afterward, as he represented his brother, King Humbert, on various official occasions when I too was present--the coronation of the Emperor Alexander of Russia, the Jubilee of Queen Victoria.
10003{_}1018{_}He was always a striking figure, didn't look as if he belonged to our modern world at all.
10003{_}1019{_}The marshal had a series of dinners and receptions which were most brilliant.
10003{_}1020{_}There was almost always music or theatricals, with the best artists in Paris.
10003{_}1021{_}The Comedie Francaise was much appreciated.
10003{_}1022{_}Their style is so finished and sure.
10003{_}1023{_}They played just as well at one end of a drawing-room, with a rampe of flowers only separating them from the public, as in their own theatre with all the help of scenery, acoustics, and distance.
10003{_}1024{_}In a drawing-room naturally the audience is much nearer.
10003{_}1025{_}I remember one charming party at the Elysee for the Austrian crown prince, the unfortunate Archduke Rudolph.
10003{_}1026{_}All the stars of the Theatre Francais were playing--Croizette, Reichemberg, Delaunay, Coquelin.
10003{_}1027{_}The prince seemed to enjoy himself.
10003{_}1028{_}He was very good-looking, with a slight, elegant figure and charming smile--didn't look like a man whose life would end so tragically.
10003{_}1029{_}When I saw him some years later in London, he was changed, looked older, had lost his gaiety, was evidently bored with the official entertaining, and used to escape from all the dinners and receptions as soon as he could.
10003{_}1030{_}The late King Edward (then Prince of Wales) won golden opinions always.
10003{_}1031{_}There was certainly something in his personality which had an enormous attraction for Parisians.
10003{_}1032{_}He always seemed to enjoy life, never looked bored, was unfailingly courteous and interested in the people he was talking to.
10003{_}1033{_}It was a joy to the French people to see him at some of the small theatres, amusing himself and understanding all the sous-entendus and argot quite as well as they did.
10003{_}1034{_}It would almost seem as if what some one said were true, that he reminded them of their beloved Henri IV, who still lives in the heart of the nation.
10003{_}1035{_}His brother-in-law, the Prince of Denmark, was also most amiable.
10003{_}1036{_}We met him often walking about the streets with one or two of his gentlemen, and looking in at the windows like an ordinary provincial.
10003{_}1037{_}He was tall, with a slight, youthful figure, and was always recognised.
10003{_}1038{_}It was a great satisfaction and pride to Parisians to have so many royalties and distinguished people among them again.
10003{_}1039{_}Those two months of May and June gave back to Paris the animation and gaiety of the last days of the Empire.
10003{_}1040{_}There were many handsome carriages on the Champs-Elysees, filled with pretty, well-dressed women, and the opera and all the theatres were packed.
10003{_}1041{_}Paris was illuminated the night of the opening of the exposition, the whole city, not merely the Champs-Elysees and boulevards.
10003{_}1042{_}As we drove across the bridge on our way home from the reception at the Elysee, it was a beautiful sight--the streets full of people waiting to see the foreign royalties pass, and the view up and down the Seine, with the lights from the high buildings reflected in the water--like fairy-land.
10003{_}1043{_}[Illustration: His Royal Highness, Edward, Prince of Wales, in 1876.
10003{_}1044{_}From a photograph by Lock & Whitfield, London.]
10003{_}1045{_}The dinners and receptions at the Elysee and at all the ministries those first weeks of the exposition were interesting but so fatiguing.
10003{_}1046{_}Happily there were not many lunches nor day entertainments.
10003{_}1047{_}I used to get a good drive every afternoon in the open carriage with mother and baby, and that kept me alive.
10003{_}1048{_}Occasionally (not often) W. had a man's dinner, and then I could go with some of my friends and dine at the exposition, which was very amusing--such a curious collection of people.
10003{_}1049{_}The rue des Nations was like a gigantic fair.
10003{_}1050{_}We met all our friends, and heard every language under the sun.
10003{_}1051{_}Among other distinguished foreign guests that year we had President and Mrs. Grant, who were received everywhere in Europe (England giving the example) like royalties.
10003{_}1052{_}When they dined with us at the Quai d'Orsay W. and I went to the top of the great staircase to meet them, exactly as we did for the Prince and Princess of Wales.
10003{_}1053{_}It seems funny to me when I think of the very unceremonious manner in which not only ex-presidents but actual presidents were treated in America when I was a child.
10003{_}1054{_}I remember quite well seeing a president (I have forgotten which one now) come into the big drawing-room at the old Cozzen's Hotel at West Point, with two or three gentlemen with him.
10003{_}1055{_}There was a certain number of people in the room and nobody moved, or dreamt of getting up.
10003{_}1056{_}However, the Grants were very simple--accepted all the honours shown to them without a pose of any kind.
10003{_}1057{_}The marshal gave them a big dinner at the Elysee.
10003{_}1058{_}We arrived a little late (we always did) and found a large party assembled.
10003{_}1059{_}The Grants came in just after us.
10003{_}1060{_}The Marechale said to me: "The Chinese ambassador will take you to dinner, Madame Waddington.
10003{_}1061{_}He is an interesting, clever man, knows England and the English well--speaks English remarkably well."
10003{_}1062{_}Just before dinner was announced the ambassador was brought up to me.
10003{_}1063{_}He was a striking-looking man, tall, broad-shouldered, dignified, very gorgeously attired in light-blue satin, embroidered in bright-coloured flowers and gold and silver designs, and a splendid yellow bird of paradise in his cap.
10003{_}1064{_}He didn't come quite up to me, made me a low bow from a certain distance, and then fell back into a group of smaller satellites, all very splendidly dressed.
10003{_}1065{_}When dinner was announced the first couples filed off--the marshal with Mrs. Grant and the Marechale with President Grant and W. with his lady.
10003{_}1066{_}There was a pause; I should have gone next, but my ambassador wasn't forthcoming.
10003{_}1067{_}I looked and wondered.
10003{_}1068{_}All the aides-de-camp were making frantic signals to me to go on, and the whole cortege was stopped.
10003{_}1069{_}I really didn't know what to do--I felt rather foolish.
10003{_}1070{_}Presently the ambassador appeared--didn't offer me his arm, but again made me a low bow, which I returned and moved a few steps forward.
10003{_}1071{_}He advanced too and we made a stately progress to the dining-room side by side.
10003{_}1072{_}I heard afterward the explanation.
10003{_}1073{_}It seemed that in those days (things have changed _now_ I fancy) no Chinese of rank would touch any woman who didn't belong to him, and the ambassador would have thought himself dishonoured (as well as me) if he had offered me his arm.
10003{_}1074{_}The dinner was anything but banal.
10003{_}1075{_}When we finally got to the table I found myself on the marshal's left--Mrs. Grant was on his right.
10003{_}1076{_}The marshal neither spoke nor understood English.
10003{_}1077{_}Mrs. Grant spoke no French, so the conversation didn't seem likely to be very animated.
10003{_}1078{_}After a few moments Mrs. Grant naturally wished to say something to her host and she addressed him in English.
10003{_}1079{_}"Mr. President, I am so happy to be in your beautiful country," then the marshal to me: "Madame Waddington je vous en prie, dites a Madame Grant que je ne puis pas repondre; je ne comprends pas l'anglais; je ne puis pas parler avec elle."
10003{_}1080{_}"Mrs. Grant, the marshal begs me to say to you that he regrets not being able to talk with you, but unfortunately he does not understand English."
10003{_}1081{_}Then there was a pause and Mrs. Grant began again: "What a beautiful palace, Mr. President.
10003{_}1082{_}It must be delightful with that charming garden."
10003{_}1083{_}Again the marshal to me: "Mais je vous en prie Madame, dites a Madame Grant que je ne puis pas causer avec elle.
10003{_}1084{_}Il ne faut pas qu'elle me parle, je ne comprends pas."
10003{_}1085{_}"Mrs. Grant, the marshal is distressed that he cannot talk to you, but he _really_ does not understand any English."
10003{_}1086{_}It was very trying for Mrs. Grant.
10003{_}1087{_}Happily her other neighbour knew a little English and she could talk to him, but all through dinner, at intervals, she began again at the marshal.
10003{_}1088{_}After a few moments I turned my attention to my ambassador.
10003{_}1089{_}I had been looking at him furtively while I was interpreting for the marshal and Mrs. Grant.
10003{_}1090{_}I saw that he _took_ everything that was offered to him--dishes, wines, sauces--but he never attacked anything without waiting to see what his neighbours did, when and how they used their knives and forks,--then did exactly as they did,--never made a mistake.
10003{_}1091{_}I saw he was looking at the flowers on the table, which were very well arranged, so I said to him, speaking very slowly and distinctly, as one does to a child or a deaf person: "Have you pretty flowers in your country?"
10003{_}1092{_}He replied promptly: "Yes, yes, very hot, very cold, very hot, very cold."
10003{_}1093{_}I was a little disconcerted, but thought I had perhaps spoken indistinctly, and after a little while I made another attempt: "How much the uniforms add to the brilliancy of the fete, and the Chinese dress is particularly striking and handsome," but to that he made such a perfectly unintelligible answer that I refrained from any further conversation and merely smiled at him from time to time, which he always acknowledged with a little bow.
10003{_}1094{_}We went back to the salons in the same way, side by side, and when the men had gone into one of the other rooms to talk and smoke, I went to speak to the Marechale, who said to me: "I am sure you had a delightful dinner, Madame Waddington.
10003{_}1095{_}The Chinese ambassador is such a clever man, has travelled a great deal, and speaks such wonderful English."
10003{_}1096{_}"Wonderful indeed, Madame la Marechale," and then I repeated our conversation, which she could hardly believe, and which amused her very much.
10003{_}1097{_}She spoke English as well as I did.
10003{_}1098{_}The Grants were very much entertained during their stay in Paris, and we met them nearly every night.
10003{_}1099{_}W. liked the general very much and found him quite talkative when he was alone with him.
10003{_}1100{_}At the big dinners he was of course at a disadvantage, neither speaking nor understanding a word of French.
10003{_}1101{_}W. acted as interpreter and found that very fatiguing.
10003{_}1102{_}There is so much repartee and sous-entendu in all French conversation that even foreigners who know the language well find it sometimes difficult to follow everything, and to translate quickly enough to keep one au courant is almost impossible.
10003{_}1103{_}When they could they drifted into English, and W. said he was most interesting--speaking of the war and all the North had done, without ever putting himself forward.
10003{_}1104{_}We had both of us often to act as interpreters with French and Anglo-Saxons, neither understanding the other's language, and always found it difficult.
10003{_}1105{_}I remember a dinner at Sandringham some years ago when W. was at the embassy.
10003{_}1106{_}The Prince of Wales (late King Edward) asked me to sit next to a foreign ambassador who understood not one word of English.
10003{_}1107{_}The dinner was exclusively English--a great many clever men--the master of Trinity College, Cambridge (asked especially to meet my husband, who graduated from Trinity College), Lord Goschen, James Knowles of the _Nineteenth Century_, Froude, the historian, Sir Henry James, Lord Wolseley, etc.
10003{_}1108{_}The talk was very animated, very witty.
10003{_}1109{_}There were peals of laughter all around the table.
