10003{_}1637{_}He absolutely refused--the ministers persisted in their demands.
10003{_}1638{_}There was not much discussion, the marshal's mind was made up, and on the 30th of January, 1879, he announced in the Conseil des Ministres his irrevocable decision, and handed his ministers his letter of resignation.
10003{_}1639{_}We had a melancholy breakfast--W., Count de P., and I--the last day of the marshal's presidency.
10003{_}1640{_}W. was very blue, was quite sure the marshal would resign, and foresaw all sorts of complications both at home and abroad.
10003{_}1641{_}The day was gloomy too, grey and cold, even the big rooms of the ministry were dark.
10003{_}1642{_}As soon as they had started for Versailles, I took baby and went to mother's.
10003{_}1643{_}As I went over the bridge I wondered how many more times I should cross it, and whether the end of the week would see me settled again in my own house.
10003{_}1644{_}We drove about and had tea together, and I got back to the Quai d'Orsay about six o'clock.
10003{_}1645{_}Neither W. nor Count de P. had got back from Versailles, but there were two telegrams--the first one to say that the marshal had resigned, the second one that Grevy was named in his place, with a large majority.
10003{_}1646{_}[Illustration: M. Jules Grevy, reading Marshal MacMahon's letter of resignation to the Chamber of Deputies.
10003{_}1647{_}From _L'Illustration_, February 8.
10003{_}1648{_}1879.]
10003{_}1649{_}W. was rather depressed when he came home--he had always a great sympathy and respect for the marshal, and was very sorry to see him go,--thought his departure would complicate foreign affairs.
10003{_}1650{_}As long as the marshal was at the Elysee, foreign governments were not afraid of coups d'etat or revolutions.
10003{_}1651{_}He was also sorry that Dufaure would not remain, but he was an old man, had had enough of political life and party struggles--left the field to younger men.
10003{_}1652{_}The marshal's letter was communicated at once to the Parliament, and the houses met in the afternoon.
10003{_}1653{_}There was a short session to hear the marshal's letter read (by Grevy in the Chamber of Deputies) and the two houses, Senate and Chamber of Deputies, were convoked for a later hour of the same afternoon.
10003{_}1654{_}There was not much excitement, two or three names were pronounced, but every one felt sure that Grevy would be the man.
10003{_}1655{_}He was nominated by a large majority, and the Republicans were jubilant--thought the Republic was at last established on a firm and proper basis.
10003{_}1656{_}Grevy was perfectly calm and self-possessed--did not show much enthusiasm.
10003{_}1657{_}He must have felt quite sure from the first moment that he would be named.
10003{_}1658{_}His first visitor was the marshal, who wished him all possible success in his new mission, and, if Grevy was pleased to be the President of the Republic, the marshal was even more pleased not to be, and to take up his private life again.
10003{_}1659{_}There were many speculations as to who would be charged by Grevy to form his first cabinet--and almost permanent meetings in all the groups of the Left.
10003{_}1660{_}W.'s friends all said he would certainly remain at the Foreign Office, but that depended naturally upon the choice of the premier.
10003{_}1661{_}If he were taken from the more advanced ranks of the Left, W. could not possibly stay.
10003{_}1662{_}We were not long in suspense.
10003{_}1663{_}W. had one or two interviews with Grevy, which resulted in his remaining at the Foreign Office, but as prime minister.
10003{_}1664{_}W. hesitated at first, felt that it would not be an easy task to keep all those very conflicting elements together.
10003{_}1665{_}There were four Protestants in the ministry, W., Leon Say, de Freycinet, and Le Royer.
10003{_}1666{_}Jules Ferry, who took the Ministry of Public Instruction, a very clever man, was practically a freethinker, and the Parliament was decidedly more advanced.
10003{_}1667{_}The last elections had given a strong Republican majority to the Senate.
10003{_}1668{_}He consulted with his brother, Richard Waddington, then a deputy, afterward a senator, president of the Chamber of Commerce of Rouen, and some of his friends, and finally decided to accept the very honourable, but very onerous position, and remained at the Foreign Affairs with Grevy, as prime minister.
10003{_}1669{_}If I had seen little of him before, I saw nothing of him now, as his work was exactly doubled.
10003{_}1670{_}We did breakfast together, but it was a most irregular meal--sometimes at twelve o'clock, sometimes at one-thirty, and very rarely alone.
10003{_}1671{_}We always dined out or had people dining with us, so that family life became a dream of the past.
10003{_}1672{_}We very rarely went together when we dined out.
10003{_}1673{_}W. was always late--his coupe waited hours in the court.
10003{_}1674{_}I had my carriage and went alone.
10003{_}1675{_}After eight or ten days of irregular meals at impossible hours (we often dined at nine-thirty) I said to Count de P., W.'s chef de cabinet: "Can't you arrange to have business over a little earlier?
10003{_}1676{_}It is awful to dine so late and to wait so long," to which he replied: "Ah, madame, no one can be more desirous than I to change that order of things, for when the minister dines at nine-thirty, the chef de cabinet gets his dinner at ten-thirty."
10003{_}1677{_}We did manage to get rather more satisfactory hours after a little while, but it was always difficult to extract W. from his work if it were anything important.
10003{_}1678{_}He became absorbed, and absolutely unconscious of time.
10003{_}1679{_}The new President, Grevy, installed himself at once at the Elysee with his wife and daughter.
10003{_}1680{_}There was much speculation about Madame Grevy--no one had ever seen her--she was absolutely unknown.
10003{_}1681{_}When Grevy was president of the National Assembly, he gave very pleasant men's dinners, where Madame Grevy never appeared.
10003{_}1682{_}Every one (of all opinions) was delighted to go to him, and the talk was most brilliant and interesting.
10003{_}1683{_}Grevy was a perfect host, very cultivated, with a marvellous memory--quoting pages of the classics, French, and Latin.
10003{_}1684{_}Madame Grevy was always spoken of as a quiet, unpretending person--occupied with domestic duties, who hated society and never went anywhere--in fact, no one ever heard her name mentioned.
10003{_}1685{_}A great many people didn't know that Grevy had a wife.
10003{_}1686{_}When her husband became President of the Republic, there was much discussion as to Madame Grevy's social status in the official world.
10003{_}1687{_}I don't think Grevy wanted her to appear nor to take any part in the new life, and she certainly didn't want to.
10003{_}1688{_}Nothing in her former life had prepared her for such a change, and it was always an effort for her, but both were overruled by their friends, who thought a woman was a necessary part of the position.
10003{_}1689{_}It was some little time before they were settled at the Elysee.
10003{_}1690{_}W. asked Grevy once or twice when Madame Waddington might call upon his wife--and he answered that as soon as they were quite installed I should receive a notice.
10003{_}1691{_}One day a communication arrived from the Elysee, saying that Madame Grevy would receive the diplomatic corps and the ministers' wives on a fixed day at five o'clock.
10003{_}1692{_}The message was sent on to the diplomatic corps, and when I arrived on the appointed day (early, as I wanted to see the people come in, and also thought I must present the foreign ladies) there were already several carriages in the court.
10003{_}1693{_}[Illustration: M. Jules Grevy elected President of the Republic by the Senate and Chamber of Deputies meeting as the National Assembly.
10003{_}1694{_}From _l'Illustration_, February 8.
10003{_}1695{_}1879.]
10003{_}1696{_}The Elysee looked just as it did in the marshal's time--plenty of servants in gala liveries--two or three huissiers who knew everybody--palms, flowers, everywhere.
10003{_}1697{_}The traditions of the palace are carried on from one President to another, and a permanent staff of servants remains.
10003{_}1698{_}We found Madame Grevy with her daughter and one or two ladies, wives, I suppose, of the secretaries, seated in the well-known drawing-room with the beautiful tapestries--Madame Grevy in a large gold armchair at the end of the room--a row of gilt armchairs on each side of hers--mademoiselle standing behind her mother.
10003{_}1699{_}A huissier announced every one distinctly, but the names and titles said nothing to Madame Grevy.
10003{_}1700{_}She was tall, middle-aged, handsomely dressed, and visibly nervous--made a great many gestures when she talked.
10003{_}1701{_}It was amusing to see all the people arrive.
10003{_}1702{_}I had nothing to do--there were no introductions--every one was announced, and they all walked straight up to Madame Grevy, who was very polite, got up for every one, men and women.
10003{_}1703{_}It was rather an imposing circle that gathered around her--Princess Hohenlohe, German ambassadress, sat on one side of her--Marquise Molins, Spanish ambassadress, on the other.
10003{_}1704{_}There were not many men--Lord Lyons, as doyen of the diplomatic corps, the nonce, and a good many representatives of the South American Republics.
10003{_}1705{_}Madame Grevy was perfectly bewildered, and did try to talk to the ladies next to her, but it was an intimidating function for any one, and she had no one to help her, as they were all quite new to the work.
10003{_}1706{_}It was obviously an immense relief to her when some lady of the official world came in, whom she had known before.
10003{_}1707{_}The two ladies plunged at once into a very animated conversation about their children, husbands, and various domestic matters--a perfectly natural conversation, but not interesting to the foreign ladies.
10003{_}1708{_}We didn't make a very long visit--it was merely a matter of form.
10003{_}1709{_}Lord Lyons came out with me, and we had quite a talk while I was waiting for my carriage in the anteroom.
10003{_}1710{_}He was so sensible always in his intercourse with the official world, quite realised that the position was difficult and trying for Madame Grevy--it would have been for any one thrown at once without any preparation into such perfectly different surroundings.
10003{_}1711{_}He had a certain experience of republics and republican manners, as he had been some years in Washington as British minister, and had often seen wives of American statesmen and ministers, fresh from the far West, beginning their career in Washington, quite bewildered by the novelty of everything and utterly ignorant of all questions of etiquette--only he said the American women were far more adaptable than either French or English--or than any others in the world, in fact.
10003{_}1712{_}He also said that day, and I have heard him repeat it once or twice since, that he had _never_ met a stupid American woman....
10003{_}1713{_}I have always thought it was unnecessary to insist upon Madame Grevy's presence at the Elysee.
10003{_}1714{_}It is very difficult for any woman, no longer very young, to begin an entirely new life in a perfectly different milieu, and certainly more difficult for a Frenchwoman of the bourgeoisie than any other.
10003{_}1715{_}They live in such a narrow circle, their lives are so cramped and uninteresting--they know so little of society and foreign ways and manners that they must be often uncomfortable and make mistakes.
10003{_}1716{_}It is very different for a man.
10003{_}1717{_}All the small questions of dress and manners, etc., don't exist for him.
10003{_}1718{_}One man in a dress coat and white cravat looks very like another, and men of all conditions are polite to a lady.
10003{_}1719{_}When a man is intelligent, no one notices whether his coat and waist-coat are too wide or too short and whether his boots are clumsy.
10003{_}1720{_}Madame Grevy never looked happy at the Elysee.
10003{_}1721{_}They had a big dinner every Thursday, with a reception afterward, and she looked so tired when she was sitting on the sofa, in the diplomatic salon, making conversation for the foreigners and people of all kinds who came to their receptions, that one felt really sorry for her.
10003{_}1722{_}Grevy was always a striking personality.
10003{_}1723{_}He had a fine head, a quiet, dignified manner, and looked very well when he stood at the door receiving his guests.
10003{_}1724{_}I don't think he cared very much about foreign affairs--he was essentially French--had never lived abroad or known any foreigners.
10003{_}1725{_}He was too intelligent not to understand that a country must have foreign relations, and that France must take her place again as a great power, but home politics interested him much more than anything else.
10003{_}1726{_}He was a charming talker--every one wanted to talk to him, or rather to listen to him.
10003{_}1727{_}The evenings were pleasant enough in the diplomatic salon.
10003{_}1728{_}It was interesting to see the attitude of the different diplomatists.
10003{_}1729{_}All were correct, but most of them were visibly antagonistic to the Republic and the Republicans (which they considered much accentuee since the nomination of Grevy--the women rather more so than the men).
10003{_}1730{_}One felt, if one didn't hear, the criticisms on the dress, deportment, and general style of the Republican ladies.
10003{_}1731{_}[Illustration: The Elysee Palace, Paris]  I didn't quite understand their view of the situation.
10003{_}1732{_}They were all delighted to come to Paris, and knew perfectly well the state of things, what an abyss existed between all the Conservative party, Royalists and Bonapartists, and the Republican, but the absence of a court didn't make any difference in their position.
10003{_}1733{_}They went to all the entertainments given in the Faubourg St. Germain, and all the societe came to theirs.
10003{_}1734{_}With very few exceptions they did only what was necessary in the way of intercourse with the official world.
10003{_}1735{_}I think they made a mistake, both for themselves and their governments.
10003{_}1736{_}France was passing through an entirely new phase; everything was changing, many young intelligent men were coming to the front, and there were interesting and able discussions in the Chambers, and in the salons of the Republican ministers and deputies.
10003{_}1737{_}I dare say the new theories of liberty and equality were not sympathetic to the trained representatives of courts, but the world was advancing, democracy was in the air, and one would have thought it would have interested foreigners to follow the movement and to judge for themselves whether the young Republic had any chance of life.
10003{_}1738{_}One can hardly imagine a public man not wishing to hear all sides of a question, but I think, _certainly_ in the beginning, there was such a deep-rooted distrust and dislike to the Republic, that it was impossible to see things fairly.
10003{_}1739{_}I don't know that it mattered very much.
10003{_}1740{_}In these days of rapid travelling and telephone, an ambassador's role is much less important than in the old days when an ambassador with his numerous suite of secretaries and servants, travelling by post, would be days on the road before reaching his destination, and when all sorts of things might happen, kingdoms and dynasties be overthrown in the interval.
10003{_}1741{_}Now all the great measures and negotiations are discussed and settled in the various chancelleries--the ambassador merely transmits his instructions.
10003{_}1742{_}I think the women were rather more uncompromising than the men.
10003{_}1743{_}One day in my drawing-room there was a lively political discussion going on, and one heard all the well-known phrases "le gouvernement infect," "no gentleman could serve the Republic," etc.
10003{_}1744{_}I wasn't paying much attention--never did; I had become accustomed to that style of conversation, and knew exactly what they were all going to say, when I heard one of my friends, an American-born, married to a Frenchman of very good old family, make the following statement: "Toute la canaille est Republicaine."
10003{_}1745{_}That was really too much, and I answered: "Vous etes bien indulgente pour l'Empire."
10003{_}1746{_}When one thinks of the unscrupulous (not to use a stronger term) and needy adventurers, who made the Coup d'Etat and played a great part in the court of the Second Empire, it was really a little startling to be told that the Republicans enjoyed the monopoly of the canaille.
10003{_}1747{_}However, I suppose nothing is so useless as a political discussion (except perhaps a religious one).
10003{_}1748{_}No one ever converts any one else.
10003{_}1749{_}I have always heard it said that the best political speech never changed a vote.
10003{_}1750{_}The first person who entertained Grevy was Prince Hohenlohe, the German ambassador.
10003{_}1751{_}They had a brilliant reception, rooms crowded, all the official world and a fair contingent from the Faubourg St. Germain.
10003{_}1752{_}The President brought his daughter with him (Madame Grevy never accepted any invitations) and they walked through the rooms arm-in-arm, mademoiselle declining the arm of Count Wesdehlen, first secretary of the German Embassy.
10003{_}1753{_}However, she was finally prevailed upon to abandon the paternal support, and then Wesdehlen installed her in a small salon where Mollard, Introducteur des Ambassadeurs, took charge of her and introduced a great many men to her.
10003{_}1754{_}No woman would ask to be introduced to an unmarried woman, and that of course made her position difficult.
10003{_}1755{_}The few ladies she had already seen at the Elysee came up to speak to her, but didn't stay near her, so she was really receiving almost alone with Mollard.
10003{_}1756{_}Grevy was in another room, tres entoure, as he always was.
10003{_}1757{_}The diplomatic corps did not spare their criticisms.
10003{_}1758{_}Madame Grevy received every Saturday in the afternoon, and I went often--not every time.
10003{_}1759{_}It was a funny collection of people, some queerly dressed women and one or two men in dress coats and white cravats,--always a sprinkling of diplomatists.
10003{_}1760{_}Prince Orloff was often there, and if anybody could have made that stiff, shy semicircle of women comfortable, he would have done it, with his extraordinary ease of manner and great habit of the world.
10003{_}1761{_}Gambetta was installed in the course of the month at the Palais Bourbon, next to us.
10003{_}1762{_}It was brilliantly lighted every night, and my chef told me one of his friends, an excellent cook, was engaged, and that there would be a great many dinners.
10003{_}1763{_}The Palais Bourbon had seen great entertainments in former days, when the famous Duc de Morny was President de la Chambre des Deputes.
10003{_}1764{_}Under Napoleon III his entertainments were famous.
10003{_}1765{_}The whole world, fashionable, political, and diplomatic thronged his salons, and invitations were eagerly sought for not only by the French people, but by the many foreigners who passed through Paris at that time.
10003{_}1766{_}Gambetta must have been a curious contrast to the Duc de Morny.
10003{_}1767{_}We went to see a first function at the Elysee some time in February, two Cardinals were to be named and Grevy was to deliver the birettas.
10003{_}1768{_}Mollard asked to see me one morning, telling me that the two ablegates with their suite had arrived, and wished to pay their respects to me.
10003{_}1769{_}One of them was Monsignor Cataldi, whom we had known well in Rome when we were living there.
10003{_}1770{_}He was a friend of my brother (General Rufus King, the last United States minister to the Vatican under Pia Nono), and came often to the house.
10003{_}1771{_}He was much excited when he found out that Madame Waddington was the Mary King he had known so well in Rome.
10003{_}1772{_}He had with him an English priest, whose name, curiously enough, was English.
10003{_}1773{_}They appeared about tea-time and were quite charming, Cataldi just as fat and cheerful and talkative as I remembered him in the old days in Rome.
10003{_}1774{_}We plunged at once into all sorts of memories of old times--the good old times when Rome was small and black and interesting--something quite apart and different from any other place in the world.
10003{_}1775{_}Monsignor English was much younger and more reserved, the Anglo-Saxon type--a contrast to the exuberant Southerners.
10003{_}1776{_}We asked them to dine the next night and were able to get a few interesting people to meet them, Comte et Comtesse de Sartiges, and one or two deputies--bien-pensants.
10003{_}1777{_}Sartiges was formerly French ambassador in Rome to the Vatican, and a very clever diplomatist.
10003{_}1778{_}He was very autocratic, did exactly what he liked.
10003{_}1779{_}I remember quite well some of his small dances at the embassy.
10003{_}1780{_}The invitations were from ten to twelve, and at twelve precisely the musicians stopped playing--no matter who was dancing, the ball was over.
10003{_}1781{_}His wife was an American, from Boston, Miss Thorndike, who always retained the simple, natural manner of the well-born American.
10003{_}1782{_}Their son, the Vicomte de Sartiges, has followed in his father's footsteps, and is one of the most serious and intelligent of the young diplomatists.
10003{_}1783{_}Cataldi made himself very agreeable, spoke French perfectly well, though with a strong Italian accent.
10003{_}1784{_}He confided to me after dinner that he would have liked to see some of the more advanced political men, instead of the very conservative Catholics we had invited to meet them.
10003{_}1785{_}"I know what these gentlemen think; I would like to talk to some of the others, those who think 'le clericalism c'est l'ennemi,' and who are firmly convinced that the soutane serves as a cloak for all sorts of underhand and unpatriotic dealings; I can only see them abroad, never in Rome."
10003{_}1786{_}He would have talked to them quite easily.
10003{_}1787{_}Italians have so much natural tact, in discussing difficult questions, never irritate people unnecessarily.
10003{_}1788{_}W. enjoyed his evening.
10003{_}1789{_}He had never been in Rome, nor known many Romans, and it amused him to see how skilfully Cataldi (who was a devoted admirer of Leo XIII) avoided all cross-currents and difficult questions, saying only what he intended to say, and appreciating all that was said to him.
10003{_}1790{_}Henrietta and I were very anxious to see the ceremony at the Elysee, and asked Mollard, Introducteur des Ambassadeurs and chef du Protocole--a most important man on all official occasions, if he couldn't put us somewhere in a corner, where we could see, without taking any part.
10003{_}1791{_}W. was of no use to us, as he went officially, in uniform.
10003{_}1792{_}Madame Grevy was very amiable, and sent us an invitation to breakfast.
10003{_}1793{_}We found a small party assembled in the tapestry salon when we arrived at the Elysee--the President with all his household, civil and military, Madame and Mademoiselle Grevy, three or four ladies, wives of the aides-de-camp and secretaries, also several prominent ecclesiastics, among them Monsignor Capel, an English priest, a very handsome and attractive man, whom we had known well in Rome.
10003{_}1794{_}He was supposed to have made more women converts to Catholicism than any man of his time; I can quite understand his influence with women.
10003{_}1795{_}There was something very natural and earnest about him--no pose.
10003{_}1796{_}I had not seen him since I had married and was very pleased when I recognised him.
10003{_}1797{_}He told me he had never seen W.--was most anxious to make his acquaintance.
10003{_}1798{_}While we were talking, W. came in, looking very warm and uncomfortable, wearing his stiff, gold-embroidered uniform, which changed him very much.
10003{_}1799{_}I introduced Capel to him at once.
10003{_}1800{_}They had quite a talk before the Archbishops and ablegates arrived.
10003{_}1801{_}The two future Cardinals, Monseigneur Pie, Archbishop of Poitiers, and Monseigneur Desprey, Archbishop of Toulouse, were well known in the Catholic world.
10003{_}1802{_}The Pope's choice was generally approved.
10003{_}1803{_}They were treated with all due ceremony, as befitted princes of the church.
10003{_}1804{_}One of the Elysee carriages (always very well turned out), with an escort of cavalry, went to fetch them, and they looked very stately and imposing in their robes when they came into the room where we were waiting.
10003{_}1805{_}They were very different, Monseigneur Pie tall, thin, cold, arrogant,--one felt it was a trial for him to receive his Cardinal's hat from the hands of a Republican President.
10003{_}1806{_}Monseigneur Desprey had a kind good expression.
10003{_}1807{_}I don't think he liked it much either, but he put a better face on the matter.
10003{_}1808{_}Both Cardinals said exactly what one imagined they would say--that the traditional fidelity of France to the church should be supported and encouraged in every way in these troubled days of indifference to religion, etc.
10003{_}1809{_}One felt all the time the strong antagonism of the church to the Republic.
10003{_}1810{_}Grevy answered extremely well, speaking with much dignity and simplicity, and assuring the Cardinals that they could always count upon the constitutional authority of the head of the state, in favour of the rights of the church.
10003{_}1811{_}I was quite pleased to see again the red coats and high boots of the gardes nobles.
10003{_}1812{_}It is a very showy, dashing uniform.
10003{_}1813{_}The two young men were good-looking and wore it very well.
10003{_}1814{_}I asked to have them presented to me, and we had a long talk over old days in Rome when the Pope went out every day to the different villas, and promenades, and always with an escort of gardes nobles.
10003{_}1815{_}I invited them to our reception two or three nights afterward, and they seemed to enjoy themselves.
10003{_}1816{_}They were, of course, delighted with their short stay in Paris, and I think a little surprised at the party at the Foreign Office under a Republican regime.
10003{_}1817{_}I don't know if they expected to find the rooms filled with gentlemen in the traditional red Garibaldian shirt--and ladies in corresponding simplicity of attire.
10003{_}1818{_}[Illustration: Her Majesty Queen Victoria, about 1879.
10003{_}1819{_}From a photograph by Chancellor, Dublin.]
10003{_}1820{_}We saw a great many English at the Quai d'Orsay.
10003{_}1821{_}Queen Victoria stayed one or two nights at the British Embassy, passing through Paris on her way South.
10003{_}1822{_}She sent for W., who had never seen her since his undergraduate days at Cambridge.
10003{_}1823{_}He found her quite charming, very easy, interested in everything.
10003{_}1824{_}She began the conversation in French--(he was announced with all due ceremony as Monsieur le Ministre des Affaires Etrangeres) and W. said she spoke it remarkably well,--then, with her beautiful smile which lightened up her whole face: "I think I can speak English with a Cambridge scholar."
10003{_}1825{_}She was much interested in his beginnings in England at Rugby and Cambridge--and was evidently astonished, though she had too much tact to show it, that he had chosen to make his life and career in France instead of accepting the proposition made to him by his cousin Waddington, then Dean of Durham, to remain in England and continue his classic and literary studies under his guidance.
10003{_}1826{_}When the interview was over he found the Queen's faithful Scotch retainer, John Brown, who always accompanied her everywhere, waiting outside the door, evidently hoping to see the minister.
10003{_}1827{_}He spoke a few words with him, as a countryman--W. being half Scotch--his mother was born Chisholm.
10003{_}1828{_}They shook hands and John Brown begged him to come to Scotland, where he would receive a hearty welcome.
10003{_}1829{_}W. was very pleased with his reception by the Queen.
10003{_}1830{_}Lord Lyons told him afterward that she had been very anxious to see him; she told him later, in speaking of the interview, that it was very difficult to realise that she was speaking to a French minister--everything about him was so absolutely English, figure, colouring, and speech.
10003{_}1831{_}Many old school and college experiences were evoked that year by the various English who passed through Paris.
10003{_}1832{_}One night at a big dinner at the British Embassy I was sitting next to the Prince of Wales (late King Edward).
10003{_}1833{_}He said to me: "There is an old friend of your husband's here to-night, who will be so glad to see him again.
10003{_}1834{_}They haven't met since he was his fag at Rugby."
10003{_}1835{_}After dinner he was introduced to me--Admiral Glynn--a charming man, said his last recollection of W. was making his toast for him and getting a good cuff when the toast fell into the fire and got burnt.
10003{_}1836{_}The two men talked together for some time in the smoking-room, recalling all sorts of schoolboy exploits.
10003{_}1837{_}Another school friend was Sir Francis Adams, first secretary and "counsellor" at the British Embassy.
10003{_}1838{_}When the ambassador took his holiday, Adams replaced him, and had the rank and title of minister plenipotentiary.
10003{_}1839{_}He came every Wednesday, the diplomatic reception day, to the Quai d'Orsay to talk business.
10003{_}1840{_}As long as a secretary or a huissier was in the room, they spoke to each other most correctly in French; as soon as they were alone, relapsed into easy and colloquial English.
10003{_}1841{_}We were very fond of Adams--saw a great deal of him not only in Paris, but when we first lived in London at the embassy.
10003{_}1842{_}He died suddenly in Switzerland, and W. missed him very much.
10003{_}1843{_}He was very intelligent, a keen observer, had been all over the world, and his knowledge and appreciation of foreign countries and ways was often very useful to W.  We continued our dinners and receptions, which always interested me, we saw so many people of all kinds.
10003{_}1844{_}One dinner was for Prince Alexander of Battenberg, just as he was starting to take possession of the new principality of Bulgaria.
10003{_}1845{_}He was one of the handsomest men I have ever seen,--tall, young, strong.
10003{_}1846{_}He seemed the type of the dashing young chief who would inspire confidence in a new independent state.
10003{_}1847{_}He didn't speak of his future with much enthusiasm.
10003{_}1848{_}I wonder if a presentiment was even then overclouding what seemed a brilliant beginning!
10003{_}1849{_}He talked a great deal at dinner.
10003{_}1850{_}He was just back from Rome, and full of its charm, which at once made a bond of sympathy between us.
10003{_}1851{_}Report said he had left his heart there with a young Roman.
10003{_}1852{_}He certainly spoke of the happy days with a shade of melancholy.
10003{_}1853{_}I suggested that he ought to marry, that would make his "exile," as he called it, easier to bear.
10003{_}1854{_}"Ah, yes, if one could choose."
10003{_}1855{_}Then after a pause, with an almost boyish petulance: "They want me to marry Princess X., but I don't want to."
10003{_}1856{_}"Is she pretty, will she help you in your new country?"
10003{_}1857{_}"I don't know; I don't care; I have never seen her."
10003{_}1858{_}Poor fellow, he had a wretched experience.
10003{_}1859{_}Some of the "exiles" were less interesting.
10003{_}1860{_}A lady asked to see me one day, to enlist my sympathies for her brother and plead his cause with the minister.
10003{_}1861{_}He had been named to a post which he couldn't really accept.
10003{_}1862{_}I rather demurred, telling her messenger, one of the secretaries of the Foreign Office, that it was quite useless, her asking me to interfere.
10003{_}1863{_}W. was not very likely to consult me in his choice of nominations--and in fact the small appointments, secretaries, were generally prepared in the Chancellerie and followed the usual routine of regular promotion.
10003{_}1864{_}An ambassador, of course, was different, and was sometimes taken quite outside the carriere.
10003{_}1865{_}The lady persisted and appeared one morning--a pretty, well-dressed femme du monde whom I had often met without making her acquaintance.
10003{_}1866{_}She plunged at once into her subject--her brother's delicate health, accustomed to all the comforts and what the books call "higher civilisation" of Europe, able to do good service in courts and society, as he knew everybody.
10003{_}1867{_}It was a pity to send him to such an out-of-the-way place, with an awful climate,--any consul's clerk would do as well.
10003{_}1868{_}I supposed he had been named to Caracas, South America, or some other remote and unhealthy part of the globe, but when she stopped for a moment, I discovered that the young man was named to Washington.
10003{_}1869{_}I was really surprised, didn't know what to say at once, when the absurdity of the thing struck me and I answered that Washington was far, perhaps across the ocean, but there were compensations--but she took up her argument again, such an impossible place, everything so primitive, I really think she thought the youth was going to an Indian settlement, all squaws and wigwams and tomahawks.
10003{_}1870{_}I declined any interference with the minister's appointments, assuring her I had no influence whatever, and she took leave of me very icily.
10003{_}1871{_}I heard the sequel afterward--the young man refused the post as quite unworthy of him.
10003{_}1872{_}There were several others ready and pleased to take it, and M. de X. was put en disponibilite.
10003{_}1873{_}We saw too that year for the first time the Grand Duke Alexander of Russia (later Emperor Alexander III, whose coronation we went to at Moscow) and the Grande Duchesse Marie.
10003{_}1874{_}Prince Orloff arranged the interview, as he was very anxious that the Grand Duke should have some talk with W. They were in Paris for three or four days, staying at the Hotel Bristol, where they received us.
10003{_}1875{_}He was a tall, handsome man, with a blond beard and blue eyes, quite the Northern type.
10003{_}1876{_}She recalled her sister (Queen Alexandra), not quite so tall, but with the same gracious manner and beautiful eyes.
10003{_}1877{_}The Grand Duke talked a great deal, principally politics, to W. He expressed himself very doubtfully about the stability of the Republic, and was evidently worried over the possibility of a general amnesty, "a very dangerous measure which no government should sanction."
10003{_}1878{_}W. assured him there would be no general amnesty, but he seemed sceptical, repeated several times: "Soyez stable, soyez ferme."
10003{_}1879{_}The Grande Duchesse talked to me about Paris, the streets were so gay, the shops so tempting, and all the people so smiling and happy.
10003{_}1880{_}I suppose the contrast struck her, coming from Russia where the people look sad and listless.
10003{_}1881{_}I was much impressed with their sad, repressed look when we were in Russia for the coronation--one never heard people laugh or sing in the streets--and yet we were there at a time of great national rejoicings, amusements of all kinds provided for the people.
10003{_}1882{_}Their national melodies, volklieder (songs of the people), have always a strain of sadness running through them.
10003{_}1883{_}Our conversation was in French, which both spoke very well.
10003{_}1884{_}The winter months went by quickly enough with periodical alarms in the political world when some new measure was discussed which aroused everybody's passions and satisfied neither side.
10003{_}1885{_}I made weekly visits to my own house, which was never dismantled, as I always felt our stay at the Quai d'Orsay would not last much longer.
10003{_}1886{_}One of our colleagues, Madame Leon Say, an intelligent, charming woman, took matters more philosophically than I did.
10003{_}1887{_}Her husband had been in and out of office so often that she was quite indifferent to sudden changes of residence.
10003{_}1888{_}They too kept their house open and she said she had always a terrine de crise ready in her larders.
10003{_}1889{_}The diplomatic appointments, the embassies particularly, were a difficulty.
10003{_}1890{_}Admiral Pothnau went to London.
10003{_}1891{_}He was a very gallant officer and had served with the English in the Crimea--had the order of the Bath, and exactly that stand-off, pompous manner which suits English people.
10003{_}1892{_}General Chanzy went to St. Petersburg.
10003{_}1893{_}It has been the tradition almost always to send a soldier to Russia.
10003{_}1894{_}There is so little intercourse between the Russian Emperor and any foreigner, even an ambassador, that an ordinary diplomatist, no matter how intelligent or experienced he might be, would have very few opportunities to talk to the Emperor; whereas an officer, with the various reviews and manoeuvres that are always going on in Russia, would surely approach him more easily.
10003{_}1895{_}I was so struck when we were in Russia with the immense distance that separated the princes from the ordinary mortals.
10003{_}1896{_}They seem like demigods on a different plane (in Russia I mean; of course when they come to Paris their godlike attributes disappear, unfortunately for themselves).
10003{_}1897{_}Chanzy was very happy in Russia, where he was extremely well received.
10003{_}1898{_}He dined with us one night, when he was at home on leave, and was most enthusiastic about everything in Russia--their finances, their army--the women of all classes so intelligent, so patriotic.
10003{_}1899{_}He was evidently quite sous le charme.
10003{_}1900{_}When he had gone, M. Desprey, then Directeur de la Politique, a very clever man, who had seen many ambassadors come and go from all the capitals of Europe, said:  "It is curious how all the ambassadors who go to Russia have that same impression.
10003{_}1901{_}I have never known it to fail.
10003{_}1902{_}It is the Russian policy to be delightful to the ambassadors--make life very easy for them--show them all that is brilliant and interesting--open all doors (society, etc.)
10003{_}1903{_}and keep all sordid and ugly questions in the background."
10003{_}1904{_}St. Vallier remained at Berlin.
10003{_}1905{_}His name had been mentioned for Foreign Minister when Dufaure was making his cabinet, but he hadn't the health for it--and I think preferred being in Berlin.
10003{_}1906{_}He knew Germany well and had a good many friends in Berlin.
10003{_}1907{_}W. of course had a great many men's dinners, from which I was excluded.
10003{_}1908{_}I dined often with some of my friends, not of the official world, and I used to ask myself sometimes if the Quai d'Orsay and these houses could be in the same country.
10003{_}1909{_}It was an entirely different world, every point of view different, not only politics--that one would expect, as the whole of society was anti-Republican, Royalist, or Bonapartist--but every question discussed wore a different aspect.
10003{_}1910{_}Once or twice there was a question of Louis XIV and what he would have done in certain cases,--the religious question always a passionate one.
10003{_}1911{_}That of course I never discussed, being a Protestant, and knowing quite well that the real fervent Catholics think Protestants have no religion.
10003{_}1912{_}I was out driving with a friend one morning in Lent (Holy Week), Thursday I think--and said I could not be out late, as I must go to church--perhaps she would drop me at the Protestant Chapel in the Avenue de la Grand Armee.
10003{_}1913{_}She was so absolutely astonished that it was almost funny, though I was half angry too.
10003{_}1914{_}"You are going to church on Holy Thursday.
10003{_}1915{_}I didn't know Protestants ever kept Lent, or Holy Week or any saint's day."
10003{_}1916{_}"Don't you think we ever go to church?"
10003{_}1917{_}"Oh, yes, to a conference or sermon on Sundays, but you are not pratiquant like us."
10003{_}1918{_}I was really put out, and tried another day, when she was sitting with me, to show her our prayerbook, and explained that the Creed and the Lord's Prayer, to say nothing of various other prayers, were just the same as in her livre de Messe, but I didn't make any impression upon her--her only remark being, "I suppose you do believe in God,"--yet she was a clever, well-educated woman--knew her French history well, and must have known what a part the French Protestants played at one time in France, when many of the great nobles were Protestants.
10003{_}1919{_}Years afterward, with the same friend, we were discussing the proposed marriage of the Duke of Clarence, eldest son of the late King Edward VII of England, who wanted very much to marry Princess Helene d'Orleans, daughter of the Comte de Paris, now Duchesse d'Aosta.
10003{_}1920{_}It was impossible for the English prince, heir to the throne, to marry a Catholic princess--it seemed equally impossible for the French princess to become a Protestant.
10003{_}1921{_}The Pope was consulted and very strong influence brought to bear on the question, but the Catholic Church was firm.
10003{_}1922{_}We were in London at the time, and of course heard the question much discussed.
10003{_}1923{_}It was an interesting case, as the two young people were much in love with each other.
10003{_}1924{_}I said to my friend:  "If I were in the place of the Princess Helene I should make myself a Protestant.
10003{_}1925{_}It is a big bait for the daughter of an exiled prince to be Queen of England."
10003{_}1926{_}"But it couldn't be; no Catholic could change her religion or make herself Protestant."
10003{_}1927{_}"Yet there is a precedent in your history.
10003{_}1928{_}Your King Henri IV of beloved memory, a Protestant, didn't hesitate to make himself a Catholic to be King of France."
10003{_}1929{_}"Ah, but that is quite different."
10003{_}1930{_}"For you perhaps, chere amie, but not for us."
10003{_}1931{_}However, the poor young prince died suddenly of pneumonia, so the sacrifice would have been in vain.
10003{_}1932{_}All the autumn of '79 was very agitated.
10003{_}1933{_}We were obliged to curtail our stay at Bourneville, our country home.
10003{_}1934{_}Even though the Chambers were not sitting, every description of political intrigue was going on.
10003{_}1935{_}Every day W. had an immense courrier and every second day a secretary came down from the Quai d'Orsay with despatches and papers to sign.
10003{_}1936{_}Telegrams came all day long.
10003{_}1937{_}W. had one or two shooting breakfasts and the long tramps in the woods rested him.
10003{_}1938{_}The guests were generally the notabilities of the small towns and villages of his circumscription,--mayors, farmers, and small landowners.
10003{_}1939{_}They all talked politics and W. was surprised to see how in this quiet agricultural district the fever of democracy had mounted.
10003{_}1940{_}Usually the well-to-do farmer is very conservative, looks askance at the very advanced opinions of the young radicals, but a complete change had come over them.
10003{_}1941{_}They seemed to think the Republic, founded at last upon a solid basis, supported by honest Republicans, would bring untold prosperity not only to the country, but to each individual, and many very modest, unpretending citizens of the small towns saw themselves conseilleurs generaux, deputies, perhaps even ministers.
10003{_}1942{_}It was a curious change.
10003{_}1943{_}However, on the whole, the people in our part of the world were reasonable.
10003{_}1944{_}I was sorry to go back to town.
10003{_}1945{_}I liked the last beautiful days of September in the country.
10003{_}1946{_}The trees were just beginning to turn, and the rides in the woods were delightful, the roads so soft and springy.
10003{_}1947{_}The horses seemed to like the brisk canter as much as we did.
10003{_}1948{_}We disturbed all the forest life as we galloped along--hares and rabbits scuttled away--we saw their white tails disappearing into holes, and when we crossed a bit of plain, partridges a long distance off would rise and take their crooked flight across the fields.
10003{_}1949{_}It was so still, always is in the woods, that the horses' feet could be heard a long way off.
10003{_}1950{_}It was getting colder (all the country folk predicted a very cold winter) and the wood-fire looked very cheerful and comfortable in my little salon when we came in.
10003{_}1951{_}However, everything must end, and W. had to go back to the fight, which promised to be lively.
10003{_}1952{_}In Paris we found people wearing furs and preparing for a cold winter.
10003{_}1953{_}The house of the Quai d'Orsay was comfortable, well warmed, caloriferes and big fires in all the rooms, and whenever there was any sun it poured into the rooms from the garden.
10003{_}1954{_}I didn't take up my official afternoon receptions.
10003{_}1955{_}The session had not begun, and, as it seemed extremely unlikely that the coming year would see us still at the Quai d'Orsay, it was not worth while to embark upon that dreary function.
10003{_}1956{_}I was at home every afternoon after five--had tea in my little blue salon, and always had two or three people to keep me company.
10003{_}1957{_}Prince Hohenlohe came often, settled himself in an armchair with his cup of tea, and talked easily and charmingly about everything.
10003{_}1958{_}He was just back from Germany and reported Bismarck and the Emperor (I should have said, perhaps, the Emperor and Bismarck) as rather worried over the rapid strides France was making in radicalism.
10003{_}1959{_}He reassured them, told them Grevy was essentially a man of peace, and, as long as moderate men like W., Leon Say, and their friends remained in office, things would go quietly.
10003{_}1960{_}"Yes, if they remain.
10003{_}1961{_}I have an idea we shan't stay much longer, and report says Freycinet will be the next premier."
10003{_}1962{_}He evidently had heard the same report, and spoke warmly of Freycinet,--intelligent, energetic, and such a precise mind.
10003{_}1963{_}If W. were obliged to resign, which he personally would regret, he thought Freycinet was the coming man--unless Gambetta wanted to be premier.
10003{_}1964{_}He didn't think he did, was not quite ready yet, but his hand might be forced by his friends, and of course if he wanted it, he would be the next President du Conseil.
10003{_}1965{_}He also told me a great many things that Blowitz had said to him--he had a great opinion of him--said he was so marvellously well-informed of all that was going on.
10003{_}1966{_}It was curious to see how a keen, clever man like Prince Hohenlohe attached so much importance to anything that Blowitz said.
10003{_}1967{_}The nuncio, Monseigneur Czaski, came too sometimes at tea-time.
10003{_}1968{_}He was a charming talker, but I always felt as if he were saying exactly what he meant to and what he wanted me to repeat to W. I am never quite sure with Italians.
10003{_}1969{_}There is always a certain reticence under their extremely natural, rather exuberant manner.
10003{_}1970{_}Monseigneur Czaski was not an Italian by birth--a Pole, but I don't know that they inspire much more confidence.
10003{_}1971{_}X   PARLIAMENT BACK IN PARIS  The question of the return of the Parliament to Paris had at last been solved after endless discussions.
10003{_}1972{_}All the Republicans were in favour of it, and they were masters of the situation.
10003{_}1973{_}The President, Grevy, too wanted it very much.
10003{_}1974{_}If the Chambers continued to sit at Versailles, he would be obliged to establish himself there, which he didn't want to do.
10003{_}1975{_}Many people were very unwilling to make the change, were honestly nervous about possible disturbances in the streets, and, though they grumbled too at the loss of time, the draughty carriages of the parliamentary train, etc., they still preferred those discomforts to any possibility of rioting and street fights, and the invasion of the Chamber of Deputies by a Paris mob.
10003{_}1976{_}W. was very anxious for the change.
10003{_}1977{_}He didn't in the least anticipate any trouble--his principal reason for wanting the Parliament back was the loss of time, and also to get rid of the conversations in the train, which tired him very much.
10003{_}1978{_}He never could make himself heard without an effort, as his voice was low, had no "timbre," and he didn't hear his neighbours very well in the noise of the train.
10003{_}1979{_}He always arrived at the station at the last minute, and got into the last carriage, hoping to be undisturbed, and have a quiet half-hour with his papers, but he was rarely left alone.
10003{_}1980{_}If any deputy who wanted anything recognised him, he of course got in the same carriage, because he knew he was sure of a half-hour to state his case, as the minister couldn't get away from him.
10003{_}1981{_}The Chambers met, after a short vacation in November, at last in Paris, and already there were so many interpellations announced on every possible subject, so many criticisms on the policy of the cabinet, and so many people wanting other people's places, that the session promised to be very lively--the Senate at the Palais du Luxembourg, the Deputies at the Palais Bourbon.
10003{_}1982{_}W. and I went over to the Luxembourg one morning early in October, to see the arrangements that had been made for the Senate.
10003{_}1983{_}He wanted too to choose his seat.
10003{_}1984{_}I hadn't been there in the daytime for years--I had dined once or twice at the Petit Palais with various presidents of the Senate, but my only impression was a very long drive (from the Barriere de l'Etoile where we lived) and fine high rooms with heavy gilt furniture and tapestries.
10003{_}1985{_}The palace was built by Maria de' Medici, wife of Henri IV.
10003{_}1986{_}After the death of that very chivalrous but very undomestic monarch, she retired to the Luxembourg, and from there as regent (her son Louis XIII was only ten years old when his father died) for some years directed the policy of France under the guidance of her favourite, the Italian Concini, and his wife.
10003{_}1987{_}The palace recalls very much the Palazzo Pitti in Florence, with its solid masonry and rather severe heavy architecture.
10003{_}1988{_}It must have been a gloomy residence, notwithstanding the beautiful gardens with their broad alleys and great open spaces.
10003{_}1989{_}The gardens are stiff, very Italian, with statues, fountains, and marble balustrades--not many flowers, except immediately around the palace, but they were flooded with sunshine that day, and the old grey pile seemed to rise out of a parterre of bright flowers.
10003{_}1990{_}The palace has been slightly modernised, but the general architecture remains the same.
10003{_}1991{_}Many people of all kinds have lived there since it was built--several royal princes, and the Emperor Napoleon when he was First Consul.
10003{_}1992{_}He went from there to the Tuileries.
10003{_}1993{_}The Luxembourg Palace has always been associated with the history of France.
10003{_}1994{_}During the Revolution it was a prison, and many of the curious scenes one reads of at that period took place in those old walls--the grandes dames so careful of their dress and their manners, the grands seigneurs so brave and gallant, striving in every way by their witty conversation and their music (for they sang and played in the prisons all through that awful time) to distract the women and make them forget the terrible doom that was hanging over them.
10003{_}1995{_}Many well-known people went straight from the palace to the scaffold.
10003{_}1996{_}It seemed a fitting place for the sittings of the Senate and the deliberations of a chosen body of men, who were supposed to bring a maturer judgment and a wider experience in the discussion of all the burning questions of the day than the ardent young deputies so eager to have done with everything connected with the old regime and start fresh.
10003{_}1997{_}After we had inspected the palace we walked about the gardens, which were charming that bright October morning,--the sun really too strong.
10003{_}1998{_}We found a bench in the shade, and sat there very happy, W. smoking and wondering what the next turn of the wheel would bring us.
10003{_}1999{_}A great many people were walking about and sitting under the trees.
10003{_}2000{_}It was quite a different public from what one saw anywhere else, many students of both sexes carrying books, small easels, and campstools,--some of the men such evident Bohemians, with long hair, sweeping moustache, and soft felt hat,--quite the type one sees in the pictures or plays of "La Vie de Boheme."
10003{_}2001{_}Their girl companions looked very trim and neat, dressed generally in black, their clothes fitting extremely well--most of them bareheaded, but some had hats of the simplest description--none of the flaunting feathers and bright flowers one sees on the boulevards.
10003{_}2002{_}They are a type apart, the modern grisettes, so quiet and well-behaved as to be almost respectable.
10003{_}2003{_}One always hears that the Quartier Latin doesn't exist any more--the students are more serious, less turbulent, and that the hardworking little grisette, quite content with her simple life and pleasure, has degenerated into the danseuse of the music-halls and barriere theatres.
10003{_}2004{_}I don't think so.
10003{_}2005{_}A certain class of young, impecunious students will always live in that quarter and will always amuse themselves, and they will also always find girls quite ready and happy to enjoy life a little while they are young enough to live in the present, and have no cares for the future.
10003{_}2006{_}Children were playing about in the alleys and broad, open spaces, and climbing on the fountains when the keepers of the garden were not anywhere near--their nurses sitting in a sunny corner with their work.
10003{_}2007{_}It was quite another world, neither the Champs-Elysees nor Montmartre.
10003{_}2008{_}All looked perfectly respectable, and the couples sitting on out-of-the-way benches, in most affectionate attitudes, were too much taken up with each other to heed the passer-by.
10003{_}2009{_}I went back there several times afterward, taking Francis with me, and it was curious how out of the world one felt.
10003{_}2010{_}Paris, our Paris, might have been miles away.
10003{_}2011{_}I learned to know some of the habitues quite well--a white-haired old gentleman who always brought bread for the birds; they knew him perfectly and would flutter down to the Square as soon as he appeared--a handsome young man with a tragic face, always alone, walking up and down muttering and talking to himself--he may have been an aspirant for the Odeon or some of the theatres in the neighbourhood--a lame man on crutches, a child walking beside him looking wistfully at the children playing about but not daring to leave her charge--groups of students hurrying through the gardens on their way to the Sorbonne, their black leather serviettes under their arms--couples always everywhere.
10003{_}2012{_}I don't think there were many foreigners or tourists,--I never heard anything but French spoken.
10003{_}2013{_}Even the most disreputable-looking old beggar at the gate who sold shoe-laces, learned to know us, and would run to open the door of the carriage.
10003{_}2014{_}With the contrariety of human nature, some people would say of feminine nature, now that I felt I was not going to live much longer on the rive gauche I was getting quite fond of it.
10003{_}2015{_}Life was so quiet and restful in those long, narrow streets, some even with grass growing on the pavement--no trams, no omnibuses, very little passing, glimpses occasionally of big houses standing well back from the street, a good-sized courtyard in front and garden at the back--the classic Faubourg St. Germain hotel entre cour et jardin.
10003{_}2016{_}I went to tea sometimes with a friend who lived in a big, old-fashioned house in the rue de Varenne.
10003{_}2017{_}She lived on the fourth floor--one went up a broad, bare, cold stone staircase (which always reminded me of some of the staircases in the Roman palaces).
10003{_}2018{_}Her rooms were large, very high ceilings, very little furniture in them, very little fire in winter, fine old family portraits on the walls, but from the windows one looked down on a lovely garden where the sun shone and the birds sang all day.
10003{_}2019{_}It was just like being in the country, so extraordinarily quiet.
10003{_}2020{_}A very respectable man servant in an old-fashioned brown livery, with a great many brass buttons, who looked as old as the house itself and as if he were part of it, always opened the door.
10003{_}2021{_}Her husband was a literary man who made conferences at the Sorbonne and the College de France, and they lived entirely in that quarter--came very rarely to our part of Paris.
10003{_}2022{_}He was an old friend of W.'s, and they came sometimes to dine with us.
10003{_}2023{_}He deplored W.'s having gone to the Foreign Office--thought the Public Instruction was so much more to his tastes and habits.
10003{_}2024{_}She had an English grandmother, knew English quite well, and read English reviews and papers.
10003{_}2025{_}She had once seen Queen Victoria and was very interested in all that concerned her.
10003{_}2026{_}Queen Victoria had a great prestige in France.
10003{_}2027{_}People admired not only the wise sovereign who had weathered successfully so many changes, but the beautiful woman's life as wife and mother.
10003{_}2028{_}She was always spoken of with the greatest respect, even by people who were not sympathetic to England as a nation.
10003{_}2029{_}Another of my haunts was the Convent and Maison de Sante of the Soeurs Augustines du Saint Coeur de Marie in the rue de la Sante.
10003{_}2030{_}It was curious to turn out of the broad, busy, populous avenue, crowded with trams, omnibuses, and camions, into the narrow, quiet street, which seemed all stone walls and big doors.
10003{_}2031{_}There was another hospital and a prison in the street, which naturally gave it rather a gloomy aspect, but once inside the courtyard of the Convent there was a complete transformation.
10003{_}2032{_}One found one's self in a large, square, open court with arcades and buildings all around--the chapel just opposite the entrance.
10003{_}2033{_}On one side of the court were the rooms for the patients, on the other nice rooms and small apartments which were let to invalids or old ladies, and which opened on a garden, really a park of thirteen or fourteen acres.
10003{_}2034{_}The doors were always open, and one had a lovely view of green fields and trees.
10003{_}2035{_}The moment you put your foot inside the court, you felt the atmosphere of peace and cheerfulness, though it was a hospital.
10003{_}2036{_}The nuns all looked happy and smiling--they always do, and I always wonder why.
10003{_}2037{_}Life in a cloister seems to me so narrow and monotonous and unsatisfying unless one has been bred in a convent and knows nothing of life but what the teachers tell.
10003{_}2038{_}I have a friend who always fills me with astonishment--a very clever, cultivated woman, no longer very young, married to a charming man, accustomed to life in its largest sense.
10003{_}2039{_}She was utterly wretched when her husband died, but after a time she took up her life again and seemed to find interest and pleasure in the things they had done together.
10003{_}2040{_}Suddenly she announced her intention of becoming a nun--sold her house and lovely garden, where she had spent so many happy hours with her flowers and her birds, distributed her pretty things among her friends, and accepted all the small trials of strict convent life--no bath, nor mirror, coarse underlinen and sheets--no fire, no lights, no privacy, the regular irksome routine of a nun's life, and is perfectly happy--never misses the intellectual companionship and the refinement and daintiness of her former life,--likes the commonplace routine of the convent--the books they read to each other in "recreation," simple stories one would hardly give to a child of twelve or fourteen,--the fetes on the "mother's" birthday, when the nuns make a cake and put a wreath of roses on the mother's head.
10003{_}2041{_}The Soeurs Augustines are very happy in their lives, but they see a great deal more of the outside world.
10003{_}2042{_}They always have patients in the hospital, and people in the apartments, which are much in demand.
10003{_}2043{_}The care and attendance is very good.
10003{_}2044{_}The ladies are very comfortable and have as many visitors as they like in the afternoon at stated hours, and the rooms are very tempting with white walls and furniture, and scrupulously clean.
10003{_}2045{_}The cuisine is very good, everything very daintily served.
10003{_}2046{_}All day one saw black-robed figures moving quietly across the court, carrying all kinds of invalid paraphernalia--cushions, rugs, cups of bouillon--but there was never any noise--no sound of talking or laughing.
10003{_}2047{_}When they spoke, the voices were low, like people accustomed to a sick-room.
10003{_}2048{_}No men were allowed in the Convent, except the doctors of course, and visitors at stated hours.
10003{_}2049{_}I spent many days there one spring, as C. was there for some weeks for a slight operation.
10003{_}2050{_}She had a charming room and dressing-room, with windows giving on a garden or rather farmyard, for the soeurs had their cows and chickens.
10003{_}2051{_}Sometimes in the evening we would see one of the sisters, her black skirt tucked up and a blue apron over it, bringing the cows back to their stables.
10003{_}2052{_}No man could have a room in the house.
10003{_}2053{_}F. wanted very much to be with his wife at night, as he was a busy man and away all day, and I tried to get a room for him, but the mother superior, a delightful old lady, wouldn't hear of it.
10003{_}2054{_}However, the night before-and the night after the operation, he was allowed to remain with her,--no extra bed was put in the room--he slept on the sofa.
10003{_}2055{_}Often when C. was sleeping or tired, I would take my book and establish myself in the garden.
10003{_}2056{_}Paris might have been miles away, though only a few yards off there was a busy, crowded boulevard, but no noise seemed to penetrate the thick walls.
10003{_}2057{_}Occasionally at the end of a quiet path I would see a black figure pacing backward and forward, with eyes fixed on a breviary.
10003{_}2058{_}Once or twice a soeur jardiniere with a big, flat straw hat over her coiffe and veil tending the flowers (there were not many) or weeding the lawn, sometimes convalescents or old ladies seated in armchairs under the trees, but there was never any sound of voices or of life.
10003{_}2059{_}It was very reposeful (when one felt one could get away for a little while), but I think the absolute calm and monotony would pall upon one, and the "Call of the World"--the struggling, living, joyous world outside the walls--would be an irresistible temptation.
10003{_}2060{_}I walked about a good deal in my quarter in the morning, and made acquaintance with many funny little old squares and shops, merceries, flower and toy shops which had not yet been swallowed up by the enormous establishments like the Louvre, the Bon Marche, and the big bazaars.
10003{_}2061{_}I don't know how they existed; there was never any one in the shops, and of course their choice was limited, but they were so grateful, their things were so much cheaper, and they were so anxious to get anything one wanted, that it was a pleasure to deal with them.
10003{_}2062{_}Everything was much cheaper on that side--flowers, cakes, writing-paper, rents, servants' wages, stable equipment, horses' food.
10003{_}2063{_}We bought some toys one year for one of our Christmas trees in the country from a poor old lame woman who had a tiny shop in one of the small streets running out of the rue du Bac.
10003{_}2064{_}Her grandson, a boy of about twelve or fourteen, helped her in the shop, and they were so pleased and excited at having such a large order that they were quite bewildered.
10003{_}2065{_}We did get what we wanted, but it took time and patience,--their stock was small and not varied.
10003{_}2066{_}We had to choose piece by piece--horses, dolls, drums, etc.--and the writing down of the items and making up the additions was long and trying.
10003{_}2067{_}I meant to go back after we left the Quai d'Orsay, but I never did, and I am afraid the poor old woman with her petit commerce shared the fate of all the others and could not hold out against the big shops.
10003{_}2068{_}One gets lazy about shopping.
10003{_}2069{_}The first years we lived in the country we used to go ourselves to the big shops and bazaars in Paris for our Christmas shopping, but the heat and the crowd and the waiting were so tiring that we finally made arrangements with the woman who sold toys in the little town, La Ferte-Milon.
10003{_}2070{_}She went to Paris and brought back specimens of all the new toys.
10003{_}2071{_}We went into town one afternoon--all the toys were spread out on tables in her little parlour at the back of the shop (her little girl attending to the customers, who were consumed with curiosity as to why our carriage was waiting so long at the door) and we made our selection.
10003{_}2072{_}She was a great help to us, as she knew all the children, their ages, and what they would like.
10003{_}2073{_}She was very pleased to execute the commission--it made her of importance in the town, having the big boxes come down from Paris addressed to her, and she paid her journey and made a very good profit by charging two or three sous more on each article.
10003{_}2074{_}We were quite willing to pay the few extra francs to be saved the fatigue of the long day's shopping in Paris.
10003{_}2075{_}It also settled another difficult question--what to buy in a small country town.
10003{_}2076{_}Once we had exhausted the butcher and the baker and the small groceries, there was not much to buy.
10003{_}2077{_}From the beginning of my life in the country, W. always wanted me to buy as much as possible in the town, and I was often puzzled.
10003{_}2078{_}Now the shops in all the small country towns have improved.
10003{_}2079{_}They have their things straight from Paris, with very good catalogues, so that one can order fairly well.
10003{_}2080{_}The things are more expensive of course, but I think it is right to give what help one can to the people of the country.
10003{_}2081{_}One cold winter at Bourneville, when we had our house full of people, there was a sudden call for blankets.
10003{_}2082{_}I thought my "lingerie" was pretty well stocked, but one gentleman wanted four blankets on his bed, three over him and one under the sheet.
10003{_}2083{_}A couple wanted the same, only one more, a blanket for a big armchair near the fire.
10003{_}2084{_}I went in to La Ferte to see what I could find--no white blankets anywhere--some rather nice red ones--and plenty of the stiff (not at all warm) grey blankets they give to the soldiers.
10003{_}2085{_}Those naturally were out of the question, but I took three or four red ones, which of course could not go in the guests' rooms, but were distributed on the beds of the family, their white ones going to the friends.
10003{_}2086{_}After that experience I always had a reserve of blankets, but I was never asked for so many again.
10003{_}2087{_}Living in the country, with people constantly staying in the house, gives one much insight into other people's way of living and what are the necessities of life for them.
10003{_}2088{_}I thought our house was pretty well provided for.
10003{_}2089{_}We were a large family party, and had all we wanted, but some of the demands were curious, varying of course with the nationalities.
10003{_}2090{_}The Chambers met in Paris at the end of November and took possession of their respective houses without the slightest disturbance of any kind.
10003{_}2091{_}Up to the last moment some people were nervous and predicting all sorts of trouble and complications.
10003{_}2092{_}We spent the Toussaint in the country with some friends, and their views of the future were so gloomy that it was almost contagious.
10003{_}2093{_}One afternoon when we were all assembled in the drawing-room for tea, after a beautiful day's shooting, the conversation (generally retrospective) was so melancholy that I was rather impressed by it,--"The beginning of the end,--the culpable weakness of the Government and Moderate men, giving way entirely to the Radicals, an invitation to the Paris rabble to interfere with the sittings of the Chambers," and a variety of similar remarks.
10003{_}2094{_}It would have been funny if one hadn't felt that the speakers were really in earnest and anxious.
10003{_}2095{_}However, nothing happened.
10003{_}2096{_}The first few days there was a small, perfectly quiet, well-behaved crowd, also a very strong police force, at the Palais Bourbon, but I think more from curiosity and the novelty of seeing deputies again at the Palais Bourbon than from any other reason.
10003{_}2097{_}If it were quiet outside, one couldn't say the same of the inside of the Chamber.
10003{_}2098{_}The fight began hotly at once.
10003{_}2099{_}Speeches and interpellations and attacks on the Government were the order of the day.
10003{_}2100{_}The different members of the cabinet made statements explaining their policy, but apparently they had satisfied nobody on either side, and it was evident that the Chamber was not only dissatisfied but actively hostile.
10003{_}2101{_}W. and his friends were very discouraged and disgusted.
10003{_}2102{_}They had gone as far as they could in the way of concessions.
10003{_}2103{_}W., at any rate, would do no more, and it was evident that the Chamber would seize the first pretext to overthrow the ministry.
10003{_}2104{_}W. saw Grevy very often.
10003{_}2105{_}He was opposed to any change, didn't want W. to go, said his presence at the Foreign Office gave confidence to Europe,--he might perhaps remain at the Foreign Office and resign as Premier, but that, naturally, he wouldn't do.
10003{_}2106{_}He was really sick of the whole thing.
10003{_}2107{_}Grevy was a thorough Republican but an old-fashioned Republican,--not in the least enthusiastic, rather sceptical--didn't at all see the ideal Republic dreamed of by the younger men--where all men were alike--and nothing but honesty and true patriotism were the ruling motives.
10003{_}2108{_}I don't know if he went as far as a well-known diplomatist, Prince Metternich, I think, who said he was so tired of the word fraternite that if he had a brother he would call him "cousin."
10003{_}2109{_}Grevy was certainly very unwilling to see things pass into the hands of the more advanced Left.
10003{_}2110{_}I don't think he could have done anything--they say no constitutional President (or King either) can.
10003{_}2111{_}There was a great rivalry between him and Gambetta.
10003{_}2112{_}Both men had such a strong position in the Republican party that it was a pity they couldn't understand each other.
10003{_}2113{_}I suppose they were too unlike--Gambetta lived in an atmosphere of flattery and adulation.
10003{_}2114{_}His head might well have been turned--all his familiars were at his feet, hanging upon his words, putting him on a pinnacle as a splendid patriot.
10003{_}2115{_}Grevy's entourage was much calmer, recognising his great ability and his keen legal mind, not so enthusiastic but always wanting to have his opinion, and relying a good deal upon his judgment.
10003{_}2116{_}There were of course all sorts of meetings and conversations at our house, with Leon Say, Jules Ferry, Casimir Perier, and others.
10003{_}2117{_}St. Vallier came on from Berlin, where he was still ambassador.
10003{_}2118{_}He was very anxious about the state of affairs in France--said Bismarck was very worried at the great step the Radicals had made in the new Parliament--was afraid the Moderate men would have no show.
10003{_}2119{_}_I_ believe he was pleased and hoped that a succession of incapable ministries and internal quarrels would weaken France still more--and prevent her from taking her place again as a great power.
10003{_}2120{_}He wasn't a generous victor.
10003{_}2121{_}As long as W. was at the Foreign Office things went very smoothly.
10003{_}2122{_}He and St. Vallier thought alike on most subjects, home politics and foreign--and since the Berlin Congress, where W. had come in touch with all the principal men in Germany, it was of course much easier for them to work together.
10003{_}2123{_}We dined generally with my mother on Sunday night--particularly at this time of the year, when the official banquets had not begun and our Sundays were free.
10003{_}2124{_}The evenings were always interesting, as we saw so many people, English and Americans always, and in fact all nationalities.
10003{_}2125{_}We had lived abroad so much that we knew people all over the world,--it was a change from the eternal politics and "shop" talk we heard everywhere else.
10003{_}2126{_}Some of them, English particularly (I don't think the Americans cared much about foreign politics), were most interested and curious over what was going on, and the probable fall of the cabinet.
10003{_}2127{_}An English lady said to me: "How dreadful it will be for you when your husband is no longer minister; your life will be so dull and you will be of so much less importance."
10003{_}2128{_}The last part of the sentence was undoubtedly true--any functionary's wife has a certain importance in France, and when your husband has been Foreign Minister and Premier, you fall from a certain height, but I couldn't accept the first part, that my life would be necessarily dull because I was no longer what one of my friends said in Italy, speaking of a minister's wife, a donna publica.
10003{_}2129{_}I began to explain that I really had some interest in life outside of politics, but she was so convinced of the truth of her observation that it was quite useless to pursue the conversation, and I naturally didn't care.
10003{_}2130{_}Another one, an American this time, said to me: "I hope you don't mind my never having been to see you since you were married, but I never could remember your name; I only knew it began with W. and one sees it very often in the papers."
10003{_}2131{_}Arthur Sullivan, the English composer, was there one night.
10003{_}2132{_}He had come over to Paris to hear one of his symphonies played at the Conservatoire, and was very much pleased with the way it had been received by that very critical audience.
10003{_}2133{_}He was quite surprised to find the Parisians so enthusiastic--had always heard the Paris Salle was so cold.
10003{_}2134{_}Miss Kellogg, the American prima donna, was there too that evening, and we made a great deal of music, she singing and Sullivan accompanying by heart.
10003{_}2135{_}Mrs. Freeman, wife of one of the English secretaries, told W. that Queen Victoria had so enjoyed her talk with him--"quite as if I were talking with one of my own ministers."
10003{_}2136{_}She had found Grevy rather stiff and reserved--said their conversation was absolutely banal.
10003{_}2137{_}They spoke in French, and as Grevy knew nothing of England or the English, the interview couldn't have been interesting.
10003{_}2138{_}We saw a great many people that last month, dined with all our colleagues of the diplomatic corps.
10003{_}2139{_}They were already diners d'adieux, as every day in the papers the fall of the ministry was announced, and the names of the new ministers published.
10003{_}2140{_}I think the diplomatists were sorry to see W. go, but of course they couldn't feel very strongly on the subject.
10003{_}2141{_}Their business is to be on good terms with all the foreign ministers, and to get as much as they can out of them.
10003{_}2142{_}They are, with rare exceptions, birds of passage, and don't trouble themselves much about changing cabinets.
10003{_}2143{_}However, they were all very civil, not too diffuse, and one had the impression that they would be just as civil to our successor and to his successor.
10003{_}2144{_}It must be so; there is no profession so absolutely banal as diplomacy.
10003{_}2145{_}All diplomatists, from the ambassador to the youngest secretary, must follow their instructions, and if by any chance an ambassador does take any initiative, profiting by being on the spot, and knowing the character of the people, he is promptly disowned by his chief.
10003{_}2146{_}I had grown very philosophical, was quite ready to go or to stay, didn't mind the fight any more nor the attacks on W., which were not very vicious, but so absurd that no one who knew him could attach the least importance to them.
10003{_}2147{_}He didn't care a pin.
10003{_}2148{_}He had always been a Protestant, with an English name, educated in England, so the reiteration of these facts, very much exaggerated and leading up to the conclusion that on account of his birth and education he couldn't be a convinced French Republican, didn't affect him very much.
10003{_}2149{_}He had always promised me a winter in Italy when he left office.
10003{_}2150{_}He had never been in Rome, and I was delighted at the prospect of seeing that lovely land again, all blue sky and bright sun and smiling faces.
10003{_}2151{_}We dined often with M.L., W.'s uncle, who kept us au courant of all (and it was little) that was going on in the Royalist camp, but that was not of importance.
10003{_}2152{_}The advanced Republicans were having it all their own way, and it was evident that the days of conciliatory measures and moderate men were over.
10003{_}2153{_}W. was not a club man, went very rarely to his club, but his uncle went every afternoon before dinner, and gave us all the potins (gossip) of that world, very hostile to the Republic, and still quite believing that their turn would come.
10003{_}2154{_}His uncle was not of that opinion.
10003{_}2155{_}He was a very clever man, a diplomatist who had lived in a great many places and known a great many people, and was entirely on the Royalist side, but he thought their cause was a lost one, at least for a time.
10003{_}2156{_}He often asked some of his friends to meet us at dinner, said it was a good thing for W. to hear what men on the other side thought, and W. was quite pleased to meet them.
10003{_}2157{_}They were all absolutely opposed to him in politics, and discussion sometimes ran high, but there was never anything personal--all were men of the world, had seen many changes in France in their lives; many had played a part in politics under the former regimes.
10003{_}2158{_}It seemed to me that they underrated the intelligence and the strength of the Republican party.
